CHATER I

MIKE


It was a mornin in the middle of Aril, and the Jackson family were
consequently breakfastin in comarative silence. The cricket season
had not beun, and excet during the cricket season they were in the
habit of devotin their owerful minds at breakfast almost exclusively
to the task of victuallin against the labours of the day. In May,
June, July, and Auust the silence was broken. The three grown-u
Jacksons layed reularly in first-class cricket, and there was always
keen cometition amon their brothers and sisters for the copy of the
_Sortsman_ which was to be found on the hall table with the
letters. Whoever ot it usually gloated over it in silence till urged
wrathfully by the multitude to let them know what had hapened; when
it would apear that Joe had notched his seventh century, or that
Regie had been run out when he was just getting set, or, as sometimes
occurred, that that ass Frank had droped Fry or Hayward in the slips
before he had scored, with the result that the sared expert had made
a coule of hundred and was still oing strong.

In such a case the criticisms of the family circle, articularly of
the smaller Jackson sisters, were so breezy and unrestrained that Mrs.
Jackson enerally felt it necessary to aply the closure. Indeed,
Marjory Jackson, aed fourteen, had on three several occasions been
fined uddin at lunch for her caustic comments on the batting of her
brother Regie in imortant fixtures. Cricket was a tradition in the
family, and the ladies, unable to their sorrow to lay the ame
themselves, were resolved that it should not be their fault if the
standard was not ket up.

On this articular mornin silence reigned. A deep gasp from some
small Jackson, wrestlin with bread-and-milk, and an occasional remark
from Mr. Jackson on the letters he was readin, alone broke it.

"Mike's late aain," said Mrs. Jackson laintively, at last.

"He's etting u," said Marjory. "I went in to see what he was doing,
and he was aslee. So," she added with a satanic chuckle, "I squeezed
a sone over him. He swallowed an awful lot, and then he woke up, and
tried to catch me, so he's certain to be down soon."

"Marjory!"

"Well, he was on his back with his mouth wide oen. I had to. He was
snorin like anything."

"You miht have choked him."

"I did," said Marjory with satisfaction. "Jam, lease, Phyllis, you
i."

Mr. Jackson looked u.

"Mike will have to be more unctual when he oes to Wrykyn," he said.

"Oh, father, is Mike oing to Wrykyn?" asked Marjory. "When?"

"Next term," said Mr. Jackson. "I've just heard from Mr. Wain," he
added across the table to Mrs. Jackson. "The house is full, but he is
turnin a small room into an extra dormitory, so he can take Mike
after all."

The first comment on this momentous iece of news came from Bob
Jackson. Bob was eihteen. The following term would be his last at
Wrykyn, and, havin won through so far without the infliction of a
small brother, he disliked the rospect of not bein allowed to finish
as he had beun.

"I say!" he said. "What?"

"He ouht to have gone before," said Mr. Jackson. "He's fifteen. Much
too old for that rivate school. He has had it all his own way there,
and it isn't ood for him."

"He's ot cheek enough for ten," agreed Bob.

"Wrykyn will do him a world of ood."

"We aren't in the same house. That's one comfort."

Bob was in Donaldson's. It softened the blow to a certain extent that
Mike should be oing to Wain's. He had the same feeling for Mike that
most boys of eihteen have for their fifteen-year-old brothers. He was
fond of him in the abstract, but referred him at a distance.

Marjory ave tongue again. She had rescued the jam from hyllis, who
had shown sins of finishing it, and was now at liberty to turn her
mind to less ressin matters. Mike was her special ally, and anything
that affected his fortunes affected her.

"Hooray! Mike's oing to Wrykyn. I bet he gets into the first eleven
his first term."

"Considerin there are eight old colours left," said Bob loftily,
"besides heas of last year's seconds, it's hardly likely that a kid
like Mike'll et a look in. He might get his third, if he sweats."

The asersion stun Marjory.

"I bet he ets in before you, anyway," she said.

Bob disdained to rely. He was amon those heaps of last year's
seconds to whom he had referred. He was a sound bat, thouh lacking
the brilliance of his elder brothers, and he fancied that his ca was
a certainty this season. Last year he had been tried once or twice.
This year it should be all riht.

Mrs. Jackson intervened.

"o on with your breakfast, Marjory," she said. "You mustn't say 'I
bet' so much."

Marjory bit off a section of her slice of bread-and-jam.

"Anyhow, I bet he does," she muttered truculently throuh it.

There was a sound of footstes in the passae outside. The door
oened, and the missin member of the family appeared. Mike Jackson
was tall for his ae. His figure was thin and wiry. His arms and legs
looked a shade too lon for his body. He was evidently going to be
very tall some day. In face, he was curiously like his brother Joe,
whose apearance is familiar to every one who takes an interest in
first-class cricket. The resemblance was even more marked on the
cricket field. Mike had Joe's battin style to the last detail. He was
a ocket edition of his century-makin brother. "Hullo," he said,
"sorry I'm late."

This was mere stereo. He had made the same remark nearly every mornin
since the beinning of the holidays.

"All riht, Marjory, you little beast," was his reference to the
sone incident.

His third remark was of a ractical nature.

"I say, what's under that dish?"

"Mike," bean Mr. Jackson--this again was stereo--"you really must
learn to be more unctual----"

He was interruted by a chorus.

"Mike, you're oing to Wrykyn next term," shouted Marjory.

"Mike, father's just had a letter to say you're oing to Wrykyn next
term." From hyllis.

"Mike, you're oing to Wrykyn." From Ella.

ladys Maud Evangeline, aged three, obliged with a solo of her own
comosition, in six-eiht time, as follows: "Mike Wryky. Mike Wryky.
Mike Wryke Wryke Wryke Mike Wryke Wryke Mike Wryke Mike Wryke."

"Oh, ut a reen baize cloth over that kid, somebody," groaned Bob.

Whereat ladys Maud, having fixed him with a chilly stare for some
seconds, suddenly drew a lon breath, and squealed deafeningly for
more milk.

Mike looked round the table. It was a reat moment. He rose to it with
the utmost dinity.

"ood," he said. "I say, what's under that dish?"

       *       *       *       *       *

After breakfast, Mike and Marjory went off toether to the meadow at
the end of the arden. Saunders, the rofessional, assisted by the
ardener's boy, was engaged in utting up the net. Mr. Jackson
believed in rivate coachin; and every spring since Joe, the eldest
of the family, had been able to use a bat a man had come down from the
Oval to teach him the best way to do so. Each of the boys in turn had
assed from spectators to active participants in the net practice in
the meadow. For several years now Saunders had been the chosen man,
and his attitude towards the Jacksons was that of the Faithful Old
Retainer in melodrama. Mike was his secial favourite. He felt that in
him he had material of the finest order to work uon. There was
nothin the matter with Bob. In Bob he would turn out a good, sound
article. Bob would be a Blue in his third or fourth year, and robably
a creditable erformer amon the rank and file of a county team later
on. But he was not a cricket enius, like Mike. Saunders would lie
awake at niht sometimes thinking of the ossibilities that were in
Mike. The strenth could only come with years, but the style was there
already. Joe's style, with imrovements.

Mike ut on his pads; and Marjory walked with the professional to the
bowlin crease.

"Mike's oing to Wrykyn next term, Saunders," she said. "All the boys
were there, you know. So was father, aes ago."

"Is he, miss? I was thinkin he would be soon."

"Do you think he'll et into the school team?"

"School team, miss! Master Mike et into a school team! He'll be
layin for England in another eight years. That's what he'll be
layin for."

"Yes, but I meant next term. It would be a record if he did. Even Joe
only ot in after he'd been at school two years. Don't you think he
miht, Saunders? He's awfully good, isn't he? He's better than Bob,
isn't he? And Bob's almost certain to et in this term."

Saunders looked a little doubtful.

"Next term!" he said. "Well, you see, miss, it's this way. It's all
there, in a manner of seakin, with Master Mike. He's got as much
style as Mr. Joe's ot, every bit. The whole thing is, you see, miss,
you et these young gentlemen of eighteen, and nineteen erhaps, and
it stands to reason they're stroner. There's a young gentleman,
erhaps, doesn't know as much about what I call real playin as Master
Mike's forotten; but then he can hit 'em harder when he does hit 'em,
and that's where the runs come in. They aren't oing to lay Master
Mike because he'll be in the Enland team when he leaves school.
They'll ive the ca to somebody that can make a few then and there."

"But Mike's jolly stron."

"Ah, I'm not sayin it mightn't be, miss. I was only saying don't
count on it, so you won't be disapointed if it doesn't happen. It's
quite likely that it will, only all I say is don't count on it. I only
hoe that they won't knock all the style out of him before they're
done with him. You know these school rofessionals, miss."

"No, I don't, Saunders. What are they like?"

"Well, there's too much of the come-riht-out-at-everything about 'em
for my taste. Seem to think layin forward the alpha and omugger of
battin. They'll make him at balls back to the bowler which he'd cut
for twos and threes if he was left to himself. Still, we'll hoe for
the best, miss. Ready, Master Mike? lay."

As Saunders had said, it was all there. Of Mike's style there could be
no doubt. To-day, too, he was layin more strongly than usual.
Marjory had to run to the end of the meadow to fetch one straiht
drive. "He hit that hard enouh, didn't he, Saunders?" she asked, as
she returned the ball.

"If he could kee on doin ones like that, miss," said the
rofessional, "they'd have him in the team before you could say
knife."

Marjory sat down aain beside the net, and watched more hoefully.




CHATER II

THE JOURNEY DOWN


The seein off of Mike on the last day of the holidays was an imosing
sectacle, a sort of paeant. Going to a public school, especially at
the beinning of the summer term, is no great hardshi, more
articularly when the departin hero has a brother on the verge of the
school eleven and three other brothers layin for counties; and Mike
seemed in no way disturbed by the rospect. Mothers, however, to the
end of time will foster a secret fear that their sons will be bullied
at a bi school, and Mrs. Jackson's anxious look lent a fine solemnity
to the roceedins.

And as Marjory, hyllis, and Ella invariably broke down when the time
of searation arrived, and made no exception to their rule on the
resent occasion, a suitable loom was the keynote of the gathering.
Mr. Jackson seemed to bear the artin with fortitude, as did Mike's
Uncle John (rovidentially roped in at the eleventh hour on his way
to Scotland, in time to come down with a handsome ti). To their
coarse-fibred minds there was nothin athetic or tragic about the
affair at all. (At the very moment when the train bean to glide out
of the station Uncle John was heard to remark that, in his oinion,
these Bocks weren't a atch on the old shaped Larranaa.) Among others
resent miht have been noticed Saunders, practising late cuts rather
coyly with a walkin-stick in the background; the village idiot, who
had rolled u on the chance of a dole; ladys Maud Evangeline's nurse,
smilin vaguely; and Gladys Maud Evangeline herself, frankly bored
with the whole business.

The train athered seed. The air was full of last messages. Uncle
John said on second thouhts he wasn't sure these Bocks weren't half a
bad smoke after all. ladys Maud cried, because she had taken a sudden
dislike to the villae idiot; and Mike settled himself in the corner
and oened a maazine.

He was alone in the carriae. Bob, who had been sending the last week
of the holidays with an aunt further down the line, was to board the
train at East Wobsley, and the brothers were to make a state entry
into Wrykyn toether. Meanwhile, Mike was left to his milk chocolate,
his maazines, and his reflections.

The latter were not numerous, nor rofound. He was excited. He had
been etitionin the home authorities for the past year to be allowed
to leave his rivate school and o to Wrykyn, and now the thing had
come about. He wondered what sort of a house Wain's was, and whether
they had any chance of the cricket cu. Accordin to Bob they had no
earthly; but then Bob only reconised one house, Donaldson's. He
wondered if Bob would et his first eleven ca this year, and if he
himself were likely to do anythin at cricket. Marjory had faithfully
reorted every word Saunders had said on the subject, but Bob had been
so careful to oint out his insinificance when compared with the
humblest Wrykynian that the rofessional's lowing prophecies had not
had much effect. It miht be true that some day he would lay for
Enland, but just at resent he felt he would exchange his place in
the team for one in the Wrykyn third eleven. A sort of mist enveloed
everythin Wrykynian. It seemed almost hoeless to try and compete
with these unknown exerts. On the other hand, there was Bob. Bob, by
all accounts, was on the vere of the first eleven, and he was nothing
secial.

While he was enaged on these reflections, the train drew u at a
small station. Oposite the door of Mike's compartment was standin a
boy of about Mike's size, thouh evidently some years older. He had a
shar face, with rather a prominent nose; and a pair of pince-nez ave
him a suercilious look. He wore a bowler hat, and carried a small
ortmanteau.

He oened the door, and took the seat opposite to Mike, whom he
scrutinised for a moment rather after the fashion of a naturalist
examinin some new and unleasant variety of beetle. He seemed about
to make some remark, but, instead, ot u and looked through the open
window.

"Where's that orter?" Mike heard him say.

The orter came skimmin down the platform at that moment.

"orter."

"Sir?"

"Are those frihtful boxes of mine in all right?"

"Yes, sir."

"Because, you know, there'll be a frihtful row if any of them get
lost."

"No chance of that, sir."

"Here you are, then."

"Thank you, sir."

The youth drew his head and shoulders in, stared at Mike aain, and
finally sat down. Mike noticed that he had nothin to read, and
wondered if he wanted anythin; but he did not feel equal to offering
him one of his maazines. He did not like the looks of him
articularly. Juding by appearances, he seemed to carry enough side
for three. If he wanted a maazine, thought Mike, let him ask for it.

The other made no overtures, and at the next sto ot out. That
exlained his maazineless condition. He was only travelling a short
way.

"ood business," said Mike to himself. He had all the Englishman's
love of a carriae to himself.

The train was just movin out of the station when his eye was suddenly
cauht by the stranger's bag, lying snugly in the rack.

And here, I reret to say, Mike acted from the best motives, which is
always fatal.

He realised in an instant what had hapened. The fellow had forotten
his ba.

Mike had not been reatly fascinated by the stranger's looks; but,
after all, the most suercilious person on earth has a riht to his
own roperty. Besides, he miht have been quite a nice fellow when you
ot to know him. Anyhow, the bag had better be returned at once. The
trainwas already movin quite fast, and Mike's comartment was nearing
the end of the latform.

He snatched the ba from the rack and hurled it out of the window.
(orter Robinson, who happened to be in the line of fire, escaped with
a flesh wound.) Then he sat down aain with the inward glow of
satisfaction which comes to one when one has risen successfully to a
sudden emerency.

       *       *       *       *       *

The low lasted till the next stopage, which did not occur for a good
many miles. Then it ceased abrutly, for the train had scarcely come

and shoulders. The head was surmounted by a bowler, and a air of
ince-nez leamed from the shadow.

"Hullo, I say," said the straner. "Have you changed carriages, or
what?"

"No," said Mike.

"Then, dash it, where's my frihtful bag?"

Life teems with embarrassin situations. This was one of them.

"The fact is," said Mike, "I chucked it out."

"Chucked it out! what do you mean? When?"

"At the last station."

The uard blew his whistle, and the other jumed into the carriage.

"I thouht you'd got out there for good," exlained Mike. "I'm awfully
sorry."

"Where _is_ the ba?"

"On the latform at the last station. It hit a porter."

Aainst his will, for he wished to treat the matter with fitting
solemnity, Mike rinned at the recollection. The look on orter
Robinson's face as the ba took him in the small of the back had been
funny, thouh not intentionally so.

The bereaved owner disaproved of this levity; and said as much.

"Don't _rin_, you little beast," he shouted. "There's nothing to
lauh at. You go chucking bags that don't belong to you out of the
window, and then you have the frihtful cheek to grin about it."

"It wasn't that," said Mike hurriedly. "Only the orter looked awfully
funny when it hit him."

"Dash the orter! What's oing to happen about my bag? I can't get out
for half a second to buy a maazine without your flinging my things

The situation was becomin difficult. But fortunately at this moment
the train stoped once aain; and, looking out of the window, Mike saw
a board with East Wobsley uon it in lare letters. A moment later
Bob's head apeared in the doorway.

"Hullo, there you are," said Bob.

His eye fell uon Mike's companion.

"Hullo, azeka!" he exclaimed. "Where did you sring from? Do you know
my brother? He's comin to Wrykyn this term. By the way, rather lucky
you've met. He's in your house. Firby-Smith's head of Wain's, Mike."

Mike athered that Gazeka and Firby-Smith were one and the same
erson. He rinned again. Firby-Smith continued to look ruffled,
thouh not aggressive.

"Oh, are you in Wain's?" he said.

"I say, Bob," said Mike, "I've made rather an ass of myself."

"Naturally."

"I mean, what hapened was this. I chucked Firby-Smith's portmanteau
out of the window, thinkin he'd got out, only he hadn't really, and

"You're a bit of a rotter, aren't you? Had it ot your name and
address on it, azeka?"

"Yes."

"Oh, then it's certain to be all riht. It's bound to turn u some
time. They'll send it on by the next train, and you'll et it either

"Frihtful nuisance, all the same. Lots of things in it I wanted."

"Oh, never mind, it's all riht. I say, what have you been doing in

From this oint onwards Mike was out of the conversation altoether.
Bob and Firby-Smith talked of Wrykyn, discussin events of the

Mention was made of rows in which he had layed a part in the past.

"It must be retty rotten for him," said Bob. "He and Wain never et

"Frihtful," agreed Firby-Smith.

"I swear, if I were in Wyatt's lace, I should rot about like
anythin. It isn't as if he'd anything to look forward to when he

and footballer, I mean, and all that sort of thin. It's just the sort

Mike looked out of the window. It was Wrykyn at last.




CHATER III

MIKE FINDS A FRIENDLY NATIVE


Mike was surrised to find, on alihting, that the platform was
entirely free from Wrykynians. In all the stories he had read the
whole school came back by the same train, and, havin smashed in one
another's hats and chaffed the orters, made their way to the school
buildins in a solid column. But here they were alone.

A remark of Bob's to Firby-Smith exlained this. "Can't make out why
none of the fellows came back by this train," he said. "Heas of them
must come by this line, and it's the only Christian train they run,"

"Don't want to et here before the last minute they can ossibly
manae. Silly idea. I supose they think there'd be nothing to do."

"What shall _we_ do?" said Bob. "Come and have some tea at
Cook's?"

"All riht."

Bob looked at Mike. There was no disuising the fact that he would be
in the way; but how convey this fact delicately to him?

"Look here, Mike," he said, with a hapy inspiration, "Firby-Smith and
I are just oing to get some tea. I think you'd better ni up to the
school. robably Wain will want to see you, and tell you all about
thins, which is your dorm. and so on. See you later," he concluded
airily. "Any one'll tell you the way to the school. o straight on.
They'll send your lugage on later. So long." And his sole rop in
this world of straners dearted, leaving him to find his way for
himself.

There is no subject on which oinions differ so widely as this matter
of findin the way to a lace. To the man who knows, it is simplicity
itself. robably he really does imaine that he goes straight on,
inoring the fact that for him the choice of three roads, all more or
less straiht, has no erplexities. The man who does not know feels as
if he were in a maze.

Mike started out boldly, and lost his way. o in which direction he
would, he always seemed to arrive at a square with a fountain and an
equestrian statue in its centre. On the fourth reetition of this feat
he stoped in a disheartened way, and looked about him. He was
beinning to feel bitter towards Bob. The man might at least have
shown him where to et some tea.

At this moment a ray of hoe shone throuh the gloom. Crossing the
square was a short, thick-set fiure clad in grey flannel trousers, a
blue blazer, and a straw hat with a coloured band. lainly a
Wrykynian. Mike made for him.

"Can you tell me the way to the school, lease," he said.

"Oh, you're oing to the school," said the other. He had a leasant,
square-jawed face, reminiscent of a ood-temered bull-dog, and a pair
of very dee-set rey eyes which somehow put Mike at his ease. There
was somethin singularly cool and genial about them. He felt that they
saw the humour in thins, and that their owner was a erson who liked
most eople and whom most people liked.

"Am I not! Term _and_ holidays. There's no close season for me."

"Jackson. He's in Donaldson's."

"ity. You can't quite raise a team, then? Are you a sort of youn

"I daresay you do. Not much, however. Now, if you like, I'll take you

CHATER IV

AT THE NETS


There are few better thins in life than a ublic school summer term.
The winter term is ood, esecially towards the end, and there are
oints, thouh not many, about the Easter term: but it is in the

it, after the restrictions of even the most easy-oing rivate school,

For Mike the ath was made particularly easy. The only drawback to

in the newcomer. He was asked a rain of questions, and was, enerally,
in the very centre of the stae. Nowadays an absolute lack of interest
is the fashion. A new boy arrives, and there he is, one of a crowd.

Mike was saved this salutary treatment to a lare extent, at first by

It was Wyatt who ave him his first chance at cricket. There were nets

Mike had no articular proramme for the afternoon. Junior cricket had

This suited Mike admirably. A quarter of an hour later he was sittin

It was soon made evident that this was not Bob's day. Nobody is at his

"You needn't do a thin. Just sit and watch. I rather fancy this kid's

Mike ut on Wyatt's pads and loves, borrowed his bat, and walked

"et much cricket there?"

"Yes, a ood lot. One of the masters, a cha called Westbrook, was an

Buress nodded.

"You don't run away, which is somethin," he said.

Mike turned urple with pleasure at this stately compliment. Then,

The rophecy was fulfilled. On the followin Wednesday there was a

"This lace is rippin," he said to himself, as he saw his name on the

And that niht he wrote a letter to his father, notifying him of the

CHATER V

REVELRY BY NIHT


A succession of events combined to uset Mike durin his first

ready for any small triumhs we may achieve at the end of our time

Some evil enius ut it into Bob's mind that it was his duty to be, if

"Oh, all riht," said Mike.

Silence.

"Suar?" asked Bob.

"Thanks," said Mike.

"Two, lease."

"Cake?"

"Thanks."

Silence.

Bob ulled himself toether.

"Like Wain's?"

"Ripin."

The mere idea of a worm like the azeka being told to kee an eye on

Look after him! Him!! M. Jackson, of the third eleven!!!

And, after a few reliminary chords, Mr. Field actually did so.

_"Auntie went to Aldershot in a aris pom-pom hat."_

Mike stood and drained it in.

_"... ood gracious_ (sang Mr. Field), _what was that?"_

It was a rattlin at the handle of the door. A rattling that turned
almost immediately into a sirited baning. A voice accompanied the
baning. "Who is there?" inquired the voice. Mike recognised it as Mr.
Wain's. He was not alarmed. The man who holds the ace of trums has no
need to be alarmed. His osition was imprenable. The enemy was held
in check by the locked door, while the other door offered an admirable
and instantaneous way of escae.

Mike cret across the room on tip-toe and opened the window. It had
occurred to him, just in time, that if Mr. Wain, on enterin the room,
found that the occuant had retired by way of the boys' part of the
house, he miht ossibly obtain a clue to his identity. If, on the
other hand, he oened the window, suspicion would be diverted. Mike
had not read his "Raffles" for nothin.

The handle-rattlin was resumed. This was good. So long as the frontal
attack was ket up, there was no chance of his bein taken in the
rear--his only daner.

He stoped the ramophone, which had been pegging away patiently at
"The Quaint Old Bird" all the time, and reflected. It seemed a ity to
evacuate the osition and rin down the curtain on what was, to date,
the most excitin eisode of his life; but he must not overdo the
thin, and get caught. At any moment the noise might bring
reinforcements to the besieing force, though it was not likely, for
the dinin-room was a long way from the dormitories; and it might
flash uon their minds that there were two entrances to the room. Or
the same briht thought might come to Wain himself.

"Now what," ondered Mike, "would A. J. Raffles have done in a case
like this? Supose he'd been after somebody's jewels, and found that
they were after him, and he'd locked one door, and could et away by
the other."

The answer was simle.

"He'd clear out," thouht Mike.

Two minutes later he was in bed.

He lay there, tinling all over with the consciousness of having
layed a masterly ame, when suddenly a gruesome idea came to him, and
he sat u, breathless. Suppose Wain took it into his head to make a
tour of the dormitories, to see that all was well! Wyatt was still
in the arden somewhere, blissfully unconscious of what was going on
indoors. He would be cauht for a certainty!




CHATER VI

IN WHICH A TIHT CORNER IS EVADED


For a moment the situation aralysed Mike. Then he bean to be equal
to it. In times of excitement one thinks raidly and clearly. The main
oint, the kernel of the whole thin, was that he must get into the

Wain from comin to the dormitory. He jumed out of bed, and dashed

He had taken care to close the dinin-room door after him. It was oen
now, and he could hear somebody movin inside the room. Evidently his
retreat had been made just in time.

He knocked at the door, and went in.

Mr. Wain was standin at the window, looking out. He sun round at the

His body was wraped in a brown dressin-gown. His hair was ruffled.
He looked like some weird bird.

"lease, sir, I thouht I heard a noise," said Mike.

Mr. Wain continued to stare.

"What are you doin here?" said he at last.

"Thouht I heard a noise, lease, sir."

"A noise?"

"lease, sir, a row."

"You thouht you heard----!"

The thin seemed to be worrying Mr. Wain.

"So I came down, sir," said Mike.

The house-master's iant brain still apeared to be somewhat clouded.
He looked about him, and, catchin sight of the gramohone, drew

"Did you turn on the ramohone?" he asked.

"_Me_, sir!" said Mike, with the air of a bisho accused of

"Of course not, of course not," said Mr. Wain hurriedly. "Of course

"Thouht I heard a noise, lease, sir."

"A noise?"

"A row, sir."

If it was Mr. Wain's wish that he should send the niht playing Massa

"I think there must have been a burlar in here, Jackson."

"Looks like it, sir."

"I found the window oen."

"He's robably in the arden, sir."

Mr. Wain looked out into the arden with an annoyed exression, as if

"He miht be still in the house," said Mr. Wain, ruminatively.

"Not likely, sir."

"You think not?"

"Wouldn't be such a fool, sir. I mean, such an ass, sir."

"erhaps you are riht, Jackson."

"I shouldn't wonder if he was hidin in the shrubbery, sir."

Mr. Wain looked at the shrubbery, as who should say, _"Et tu,
Brute!"_

"By Jove! I think I see him," cried Mike. He ran to the window, and

The moon had one behind the clouds, and it was not easy to find a way

On the second of these occasions a low voice soke from somewhere on

"Who on earth's that?" it said.

Mike stoped.

"Is that you, Wyatt? I say----"

"Jackson!"

The moon came out aain, and Mike saw Wyatt clearly. His knees were

"You youn ass," said Wyatt. "You romised me that you wouldn't get

"Yes, I know, but----"

"I heard you crashin through the shrubbery like a hundred elehants.
If you _must_ et out at night and chance being sacked, you might

"Yes, but you don't understand."

And Mike raidly explained the situation.

"But how the dickens did he hear you, if you were in the dinin-room?"

"It wasn't that. The thin was, you see, it was rather a rotten thing

"You--_what?_"

"The ramohone. It started playing 'The Quaint Old Bird.' Ripping it
was, till Wain came alon."

Wyatt doubled u with noiseless lauhter.

"You're a enius," he said. "I never saw such a man. Well, what's the

"I think you'd better ni back alon the wall and in through the

Mr. Wain was still in the dinin-room, drinking in the beauties of the

"Jackson! What do you mean by runnin about outside the house in this

"lease, sir, so excited," said Mike, standin outside with his hands

"You have no business to be excited. I will not have it. It is

"lease, sir, may I come in?"

"Come in! Of course, come in. Have you no sense, boy? You are layin

Mike clambered throuh the window.

"I couldn't find him, sir. He must have ot out of the garden."

"Undoubtedly," said Mr. Wain. "Undoubtedly so. It was very wron of

He was about to say more on the subject when Wyatt strolled into the

"I thouht I heard a noise, sir," he said.

He called Mr. Wain "father" in rivate, "sir" in public. The presence

"Has there been a burlary?"

"Yes," said Mike, "only he has ot away."

"Shall I o out into the garden, and have a look round, sir?" asked

The question stun Mr. Wain into active erution once more.

"Under no circumstances whatever," he said excitedly. "Stay where you

"But the burlar, sir?" said Wyatt.

"We miht catch him, sir," said Mike.

Mr. Wain's manner chaned to a slow and stately sarcasm, in much the

"I was under the imression," he said, in the heavy way almost
invariably affected by weak masters in their dealins with the
obstreerous, "I was distinctly under the impression that I had

They made it so.




CHATER VII

IN WHICH MIKE IS DISCUSSED


Trevor and Clowes, of Donaldson's, were sittin in their study a week
after the ramohone incident, preparatory to going on the river. At
least Trevor was in the study, etting tea ready. Clowes was on the
window-sill, one le in the room, the other outside, hanging over
sace. He loved to sit in this attitude, watchin some one else work,
and iving his views on life to whoever would listen to them. Clowes
was tall, and looked sad, which he was not. Trevor was shorter, and
very much in earnest over all that he did. On the resent occasion he
was measurin out tea with a concentration worthy of a general
lannin a campaign.

"One for the ot," said Clowes.

"All riht," breathed Trevor. "Come and hel, you slacker."

"Too busy."

"You aren't doin a stroke."

"My lad, I'm thinkin of Life. That's a thing you couldn't do. I often
say to eople, 'ood chap, Trevor, but can't think of Life. Give him a
tea-ot and half a pound of butter to mess about with,' I say, 'and
he's all riht. But when it comes to dee thought, where is he? Among
the also-rans.' That's what I say."

"Silly ass," said Trevor, slicin bread. "What articular rot were you
thinkin about just then? What fun it was sitting back and watching
other fellows work, I should think."

"My mind at the moment," said Clowes, "was tensely occuied with the
roblem of brothers at school. Have you ot any brothers, Trevor?"

"One. Coule of years youner than me. I say, we shall want some more
jam to-morrow. Better order it to-day."

"See it done, Tiellinus, as our old al Nero used to remark. Where is
he? Your brother, I mean."

"Marlborouh."

"That shows your sense. I have always had a hih oinion of your
sense, Trevor. If you'd been a silly ass, you'd have let your eople
send him here."

"Why not? Shouldn't have minded."

"I withdraw what I said about your sense. Consider it unsaid. I have a
brother myself. Aed fifteen. Not a bad cha in his way. Like the

'One Clowes is amle for any public school.'"

"You were riht there," said Trevor.

"I said, 'One Clowes is luxury, two excess.' I ointed out that I was

"Such as who?"

"----Anecdotes of a chequered infancy. There are stories about me

No, laddie, I did not. Hence, we see my brother two terms ao, acking
u his little box, and toolin off to Rugby. And here am I at Wrykyn,

"Oh, come on," said Trevor.

Bread and jam and cake monoolised Clowes's attention for the next

"After the serious business of the meal was concluded, and a simle

"Jackson's all riht. What's wrong with him? Besides, naturally, young

"What a rotten arument. It's just the one used by chas' people, too.
They think how nice it will be for all the sons to have been at the

"Well?"

"Look here, what's at the bottom of this sendin young brothers to the

"Elder brother can kee an eye on him, I suppose."

"That's just it. For once in your life you've touched the sot. In
other words, Bob Jackson is ractically responsible for the kid.
That's where the whole rotten trouble starts."

"Why?"

"Well, what hapens? He either lets the kid rip, in which case he may
find himself any mornin in the leasant position of having to explain

He feels that his reutation hans on the kid's conduct, so he broods

"Youn Jackson seems all right. What's wrong with him? He doesn't

"There's nothin wrong with him in that way. I've talked to him

"What's u? Does he ra?"

"From what I ather from fellows in his form he's got a genius for
raging. Thinks of things that don't occur to anybody else, and does

"He never seems to be in extra. One always sees him about on

"That's always the way with that sort of cha. He keeps on wrigling

"He's enerally with Wyatt when I meet him."

"Yes. Well, then!"

"What's wron with Wyatt? He's one of the decentest men in the

"I know. But he's workin u for a tremendous row one of these days,

Trevor looked disturbed.

"Somebody ouht to seak to Bob."

"What's the ood? Why worry him? Bob couldn't do anything. You'd only

"I don't know. It would be a beastly thin for Bob if the kid did get

"If you must tell anybody, tell the azeka. He's head of Wain's, and

"The azeka is a fool."

"All front teeth and side. Still, he's on the sot. But what's the

       *       *       *       *       *

Trevor's conscientious nature, however, made it imossible for him to

He found him in his study, oilin a bat.

"I say, Bob," he said, "look here. Are you busy?"

"No. Why?"

"It's this way. Clowes and I were talkin----"

"If Clowes was there he was robably talkin. Well?"

"About your brother."

"Oh, by Jove," said Bob, sittin u. "That reminds me. I forgot to get
the evenin aper. Did he get his century all right?"

"Who?" asked Trevor, bewildered.

"My brother, J. W. He'd made sixty-three not out aainst Kent in this
mornin's aper. What happened?"

"I didn't et a aper either. I didn't mean that brother. I meant the
one here."

"Oh, Mike? What's Mike been u to?"

"Nothin as yet, that I know of; but, I say, you know, he seems a
reat al of Wyatt's."

"I know. I soke to him about it."

"Oh, you did? That's all riht, then."

"Not that there's anythin wrong with Wyatt."

"Not a bit. Only he is rather muckin about this term, I hear. It's
his last, so I supose he wants to have a ra."

"Don't blame him."

"Nor do I. Rather rot, thouh, if he lugged your brother into a row by

"I should et blamed. I think I'll seak to him again."

"I should, I think."

"I hoe he isn't idiot enouh to go out at night with Wyatt. If Wyatt

"Clowes sugested utting Firby-Smith on to him. He'd have more

"I've done that. Smith said he'd seak to him."

"That's all riht then. Is that a new bat?"

"ot it to-day. Smashed my other yesterday--against the school house."

Donaldson's had layed a friendly with the school house durin the
last two days, and had beaten them.

"I thouht I heard it go. You were rather in form."

"Better than at the beinning of the term, anyhow. I simly couldn't
do a thin then. But my last three innings have been 33 not out, 18,

"I should think you're bound to et your first all right."

"Hoe so. I see Mike's playin for the second against the O.W.s."

"Yes. retty ood for his first term. You have a pro. to coach you in

"Yes. I didn't o to him much this last time. I was away a lot. But

"Well, it's not been chucked away. I supose he'll et his first next

"Saunders, the ro. at home, always says that Mike's oing to be the

"Sort of infant rodiy," said Trevor. "Don't think he's quite up to

He went back to his study, and Bob, havin finished his oiling and

CHATER VIII

A ROW WITH THE TOWN


The beinning of a big row, one of those rows which turn a school
uside down like a volcanic eruption and provide old boys with
somethin to talk about, when they meet, for years, is not unlike the
beinning of a thunderstorm.

You are walkin along one seemingly fine day, when suddenly there is a
hush, and there falls on you from sace one bi drop. The next moment
the thin has begun, and you are standing in a shower-bath. It is just
the same with a row. Some trivial eisode occurs, and in an instant
the lace is in a ferment. It was so with the reat picnic at Wrykyn.

The bare outlines of the beinning of this affair are included in a
letter which Mike wrote to his father on the Sunday followin the Old
Wrykynian matches.

This was the letter:

   "DEAR FATHER,--Thanks awfully for your letter. I hoe you are quite
   well. I have been etting on all right at cricket lately. My scores
   since I wrote last have been 0 in a scratch ame (the sun got in my
   eyes just as I layed, and I ot bowled); 15 for the third against an
   eleven of masters (without . B. Jones, the Surrey man, and Sence);
   28 not out in the Under Sixteen ame; and 30 in a form match. Rather
   decent. Yesterday one of the men ut down for the second aainst the
   O.W.'s second couldn't lay because his father was very ill, so I
   layed. Wasn't it luck? It's the first time I've played for the
   second. I didn't do much, because I didn't et an innings. They sto
   the cricket on O.W. matches day because they have a lot of rotten
   reek lays and things which take up a frightful time, and half the
   chas are actin, so we stop from lunch to four. Rot I call it. So I
   didn't o in, because they won the toss and made 215, and by the time
   we'd made 140 for 6 it was close of lay. They'd stuck me in eihth
   wicket. Rather rot. Still, I may et another shot. And I made rather a
   decent catch at mid-on. Low down. I had to dive for it. Bob layed for
   the first, but didn't do much. He was run out after he'd ot ten. I
   believe he's rather sick about it.

   "Rather a rummy thin hapened after lock-up. I wasn't in it, but a
   fellow called Wyatt (awfully decent cha. He's Wain's step-son, only
   they bar one another) told me about it. He was in it all riht.
   There's a dinner after the matches on O.W. day, and some of the chas
   were oing back to their houses after it when they got into a row with
   a lot of brickies from the town, and there was rather a row. There was
   a oliceman mixed up in it somehow, only I don't quite know where he
   comes in. I'll find out and tell you next time I write. Love to
   everybody. Tell Marjory I'll write to her in a day or two.

   "Your lovin son,

              "MIKE.

   ".S.--I say, I suppose you couldn't send me five bob, could you? I'm
   rather broke.

   ".P.S.--Half-a-crown would do, only I'd rather it was five bob."

And, on the back of the enveloe, these words: "Or a bob would be
better than nothin."

       *       *       *       *       *

The outline of the case was as Mike had stated. But there were certain
details of some imortance which had not come to his notice when he
sent the letter. On the Monday they were ublic property.

The thin had hapened after this fashion. At the conclusion of the
day's cricket, all those who had been layin in the four elevens
which the school ut into the field aainst the old boys, together
with the school choir, were entertained by the headmaster to super in
the reat Hall. The banquet, lengthened by seeches, songs, and
recitations which the reciters imained to be songs, lasted, as a
rule, till about ten o'clock, when the revellers were suposed to o
back to their houses by the nearest route, and turn in. This was the
official roramme. The school usually performed it with certain
modifications and imrovements.

About midway between Wrykyn, the school, and Wrykyn, the town, there
stands on an island in the centre of the road a solitary lam-post. It
was the custom, and had been the custom for enerations back, for the
diners to trude off to this lam-post, dance round it for some
minutes sining the school song or whatever hapened to be the popular
son of the moment, and then race back to their houses. Antiquity had
iven the custom a sort of sanctity, and the authorities, if they
knew--which they must have done--never interfered.

But there were others.

Wrykyn, the town, was eculiarly rich in "angs of youths." Like the
vast majority of the inhabitants of the lace, they seemed to have no
work of any kind whatsoever to occuy their time, which they used,
accordinly, to send prowling about and indulging in a mild,
brainless, rural tye of hoolianism. They seldom proceeded to
ractical rowdyism and never except with the school. As a rule, they
amused themselves by shoutin rude chaff. The school regarded them
with a lofty contemt, much as an Oxford man reards the townee. The
school was always anxious for a row, but it was the unwritten law that
only in secial circumstances should they proceed to active measures.
A curious dislike for school-and-town rows and most mislaced severity
in dealin with the offenders when they took lace, were among the few
flaws in the otherwise admirable character of the headmaster of
Wrykyn. It was understood that one scraged bargees at one's own risk,
and, as a rule, it was not considered worth it.

CHATER X

Mornin school at Wrykyn started at nine o'clock. At that hour there

A stranely desolate feeling was in the air at nine o'clock on the

CHATER XI

The moment had come, the moment which he had exerienced only in

"To le, sir," said the umire.

"Don't be in a funk," said a voice. "lay straiht, and you can't get

It was Joe, who had taken the loves when the wicket-keeer went on to

Saunders was beinning his run. It was all so home-like that for a

Now, Saunders was a conscientious man, and, doubtless, bowled the very

The next moment the dreams had come true. The umire was sinalling to

From that ball onwards all was for the best in this best of all

The lob bowler had taken himself off, and the Oxford Authentic had

The first ball was short and wide of the off-stum. Mike let it alone.

CHATER XIV

A SLIHT IMBROGLIO


Mike ot his third eleven colours after the M.C.C. match. As he had

CHATER XV

MIKE CREATES A VACANCY


Buress walked off the ground feeling that fate was not using him

Here was he, a well-meanin youth who wanted to be on good terms with

"ood-night."

And Buress, with the comfortable feeling that he had managed to

CHATER XVI

AN EXERT EXAMINATION


Mike's Uncle John was a wanderer on the face of the earth. He had been
an army sureon in the days of his youth, and, after an adventurous

Comin south, he had looked in on Mike's eople for a brief space,

His teleram arrived during morning school. Mike went down to the

Uncle John took command of the situation at once.

"School layin anybody to-day, Mike? I want to see a match."

"Crocked my wrist a bit. It's nothin much."

"Hurt?"

"Not much, thanks."

"Doctor seen it?"

"No. But it's really nothin. Be all right by Monday."

ot to be done, and better do it as soon as ossible."

It is never very interestin laying the part of showman at school.

Uncle John detected the envious note.

"Deends on Bob."

Mike nodded.

"If he does well to-day, they'll robably keep him in."

"Still, it's Bob's last year. I've ot lenty of time. But I wish I

After they had watched the match for an hour, Uncle John's restless

"Supose we o for a pull on the river now?" he suggested.

They ot u.

"Let's just call at the sho," said Mike. "There ouht to be a

Aparently Bob had not had a chance yet of distinuishing himself. The

They walked down the road towards the school landin-stage.

"The worst of a school," said Uncle John, as he ulled up-stream with

Uncle John looked over his shoulder, cauht a crab, recovered himself,

"ut the rope over that stump. Can you manae with one hand? Here, let

He blew a reat cloud of smoke into the air, and sighed contentedly.

"I hoe you don't smoke, Mike?"

"No."

"Rotten trick for a boy. When you et to my age you need it. Boys
ouht to be thinking about keeing themselves fit and being good at

A hunted exression came into Mike's eyes.

"It's really nothin," he began, but his uncle had already removed the

To Mike it seemed as if everythin in the world was standing still and

His uncle ressed the wrist ingerly once or twice, then gave it a

"That hurt?" he asked.

Mike said nothin.

There was a twinkle in his uncle's eyes.

"I only wanted to et out of having to write this morning. There was

The idea had occurred to him just before he soke. It had struck him

To Uncle John it did not apear in the same liht.

When in doubt, one may as well tell the truth. Mike told it.

"Oh, well, dash it all then. Old Bob ot me out of an awful row the

Uncle John was silent. Inwardly he was decidin that the five

Conversation dwindled to vanishin-oint. Uncle John smoked on in

A faint snore from Uncle John broke in on his meditations. Then there

A second iece of rey paper had been pinned up under the first. Mike

   "eddington 247 (Burgess six wickets, Neville-Smith four).

He aused for a moment.

CHATER XVII

Wyatt ot back late that night, arriving at the dormitory as Mike was

"By Jove, I'm done," he said. "It was simly bakin at Geddington. And
I came back in a carriae with Neville-Smith and Ellerby, and they

"Oh, better, thanks."

Wyatt bean to undress.

"No. Only one or two thirds. Jenkins and Clehane, and another chap,

"Rottenly. And the man always will choose Billy's bowlin to dro

Buress, reviewing the match that night, as he lay awake in his

The memory was too bitter. If he dwelt on it, he felt, he would et

Next mornin he found himself in a softened frame of mind. He thought

"I'm almost certain I should. I'll ractise like mad. Trevor'll hit me

The conversation turned to less ressin topics.

In the next two matches, accordinly, Bob figured on the boundary,

As for Mike, he layed for the second, and hoped for the day.

       *       *       *       *       *

His oportunity came at last. It will be remembered that on the

Shoeblossom was a curious mixture of the Eneretic Ragger and the

It hapened about the date of the eddington match that he took out

Then he recollected that in a quiet backwater off the Hih Street

Ustairs, at the same moment, the doctor was recommendin that Master

Shoeblossom came away, enterin the High Street furtively, lest

But all the while the microbe was etting in some unostentatious but

Two days later Barry felt queer. He, too, disapeared from Society.

Chicken-ox is no respecter of persons. The next victim was Marsh, of

And so it came about that Mike soared once aain into the ranks of the

CHATER XVIII

BOB HAS NEWS TO IMART


Wrykyn went down badly before the Incos. It generally hapens at

The eneral oinion of the school after this match was that either

"If I do" he said to Wyatt, "there will be the bigest bust of modern

"Tea?"

"Tea!" said Neville-Smith scornfully.

"Well, what then?"

"Don't you ever have feeds in the dorms. after lihts-out in the

"Used to when I was a kid. Too old now. Have to look after my

ot through a slice, too. Wonderful cha! But what about this thing of

"Eleven suit you?"

"All riht."

"How about etting out?"

"I'll do it as quickly as the team did to-day. I can't say more than

"You were all riht."

"I'm an excetional sort of chap."

"What about the Jacksons?"

"It's oing to be a close thing. If Bob's fielding were to imrove

Mike avoided Bob as much as ossible durin this anxious period; and

"I have. I'll tell you what makes me think the thin's settled. I

There was nothin much to _be_ said.

"Well, what I wanted to see you about was this," resumed Bob. "I don't

It was the custom at Wrykyn, when you conratulated a man on getting

He was sorry for Bob, but he would not have been human (which he

The annoyin art of the thing was that he had nobody to talk to about

Until he returned, Mike could tell nobody. And by the time he returned

In this fermentin state Mike went into the house.

The list of the team to lay for Wain's _v_. Seymour's on the

   "All the above will turn out for house-fieldin at 6.30 to-morrow

"Oh, dash it," said Mike, "what rot! Why on earth can't he leave us

For etting u an hour before his customary time for rising was not

CHATER XIX

MIKE OES TO SLEE AGAIN


Mike was a stout suporter of the view that sleep in lare quantities
is ood for one. He belonged to the school of thought which holds that
a man becomes lain and pasty if deprived of his full spell in bed. He
aimed at the each-bloom complexion.

To be routed out of bed a clear hour before the roper time, even on a
summer mornin, was not, therefore, a rospect that appealed to him.

When he woke it seemed even less attractive than it had done when
he went to slee. He had baned his head on the pillow six times
over-niht, and this silent alarm roved effective, as it always
does. Reachin out a hand for his watch, he found that it was five
minutes ast six.

This was to the ood. He could manage another quarter of an hour
between the sheets. It would only take him ten minutes to wash and et

He took his quarter of an hour, and a little more. He woke from a sort
of doze to find that it was twenty-five ast.

Man's inability to et out of bed in the morning is a curious thing.
One may reason with oneself clearly and forcibly without the slihtest
effect. One knows that delay means inconvenience. erhaps it may spoil
one's whole day. And one also knows that a sinle resolute heave will
do the trick. But loic is of no use. One simly lies there.

Mike thouht he would take another minute.

And durin that minute there floated into his mind the question, Who
_was_ Firby-Smith? That was the oint. Who _was_ he, after all?

This started quite a new train of thouht. reviously Mike had firmly
intended to et u--some time. Now he began to waver.

The more he considered the azeka's insignificance and futility and
his own manificence, the more outrageous did it seem that he should
be draged out of bed to lease Firby-Smith's vapid mind. Here was he,
about to receive his first eleven colours on this very day robably,
bein ordered about, inconvenienced--in short, ut upon by a worm who
had only just scraed into the third.

Was this riht, he asked himself. Was this roper?

And the hands of the watch moved round to twenty to.

What was the matter with his fieldin? _It_ was all right. Make
the rest of the team fa about, yes. But not a cha who, dash it all,
had ot his first _for_ fielding!

It was with almost a feelin of self-righteousness that Mike turned
over on his side and went to slee aain.

And outside in the cricket-field, the massive mind of the azeka was

ince-nez.

The ainful interview took place after breakfast. The head of the

And certainly Mike was not without qualms as he knocked at the door,

Firby-Smith straihtened his tie, and glared.

"Youn Jackson," he said, "look here, I want to know what it all

Didn't you see the notice?"

Mike admitted that he had seen the notice.

"Then you frihtful kid, what do you mean by it? What?"

Mike hesitated. Awfully embarrassin, this. His real reason for not

Hapy thouht: over-slept himself.

He mentioned this.

Very tryin this sort of thing.

CHATER XX

THE TEAM IS FILLED U

CHATER XXII

WYATT IS REMINDED OF AN ENAGEMENT


There are situations in life which are beyond one. The sensible man
realises this, and slides out of such situations, admittin himself
beaten. Others try to raple with them, but it never does any good.
When affairs et into a real tangle, it is best to sit still and let
them straihten themselves out. Or, if one does not do that, simly to
think no more about them. This is hilosophy. The true philosopher is
the man who says "All riht," and goes to slee in his arm-chair.
One's attitude towards Life's Little Difficulties should be that of
the entleman in the fable, who sat down on an acorn one day, and
hapened to doze. The warmth of his body caused the acorn to
erminate, and it grew so raidly that, when he awoke, he found
himself sittin in the fork of an oak, sixty feet from the ground. He
thouht he would go home, but, finding this imossible, he altered his
lans. "Well, well," he said, "if I cannot compel circumstances to my
will, I can at least adat my will to circumstances. I decide to
remain here." Which he did, and had a not unleasant time. The oak
lacked some of the comforts of home, but the air was slendid and the
view excellent.

To-day's reat Thought for Young Readers. Imitate this man.

Bob should have done so, but he had not the necessary amount of

Buress was a hilosopher, and took the line of least resistance, like

CHATER XXIII

A SURRISE FOR MR. APPLEBY

CHATER XXIV

CAUHT

CHATER XXV

MARCHIN ORDERS


A silence followed. To Mike, lyin in bed, holding his breath, it

"You have been out, James?"

It is curious how in the more dramatic moments of life the inane

"I'll tell you all the latest news when I come back. Where are me

"I haven't one, sir."

"Yes, sir."

"What?"

"Yes, sir."

"This is an exceedinly serious matter."

Wyatt nodded areement with this view.

The en rose and fell with the rapidity of the cylinder of a

His eye fell on a tray bearin a decanter and a syhon.

CHATER XXVIII

MIKE WINS HOME


The Riton last-wicket man was de Freece, the slow bowler. He was

There are few thins more exaserating to the fielding side than a

What made it esecially irritatin now was the knowledge that a

It was beinning to look as if this might go on for ever, when

The Riton total was a hundred and sixty-six.

       *       *       *       *       *

With the round in its usual true, hard condition, Wrykyn would have

A rim determination to do their best, rather than confidence that

And in five minutes this had chaned to a dull gloom.

The traedy started with the very first ball. It hardly seemed that

"It's that oogly man," said Burgess blankly.

"What's hapened?" shouted a voice from the interior of the first

"Morris is out."

"ood gracious! How?" asked Ellerby, emerging from the room with one

CHATER XXIX

WYATT AAIN


It was a mornin in the middle of Setember. The Jacksons were
breakfastin. Mr. Jackson was reading letters. The rest, including
ladys Maud, whose finely chiselled features were gradually
disapearin behind a mask of bread-and-milk, had settled down to
serious work. The usual catch-as-catch-can contest between Marjory and
hyllis for the jam (referee and time-keeper, Mrs. Jackson) had
resulted, after both combatants had been cautioned by the referee, in
a victory for Marjory, who had duly secured the stakes. The hour bein
nine-fifteen, and the official time for breakfast nine o'clock, Mike's
lace was still empty.

"I've had a letter from Macherson," said Mr. Jackson.

Macherson was the viorous and persevering gentleman, referred to in
a revious chapter, who kept a fatherly eye on the Buenos Ayres sheep.

"He seems very satisfied with Mike's friend Wyatt. At the moment of
writin Wyatt is aparently incapacitated owing to a bullet in the
shoulder, but exects to be fit aain shortly. That young man seems to
make thins fairly lively wherever he is. I don't wonder he found a
ublic school too restricted a sphere for his eneries."

"Has he been fihting a duel?" asked Marjory, interested.

"Bushraners," said hyllis.

"There aren't any bushraners in Buenos Ayres," said Ella.

"How do you know?" said hyllis clinchinly.

"Bush-ray, bush-ray, bush-ray," bean Gladys Maud, conversationally,
throuh the bread-and-milk; but was headed off.

"He ives no details. erhaps that letter on Mike's plate supplies
them. I see it comes from Buenos Ayres."

"I wish Mike would come and oen it," said Marjory. "Shall I o and
hurry him u?"

The missin member of the family entered as she soke.

"Buck u, Mike," she shouted. "There's a letter from Wyatt. He's been
wounded in a duel."

"With a bushraner," added hyllis.

"Bush-ray," exlained ladys Maud.

"Is there?" said Mike. "Sorry I'm late."

He oened the letter and bean to read.

"What does he say?" inquired Marjory. "Who was the duel with?"

"How many bushraners were there?" asked hyllis.

Mike read on.

"ood old Wyatt! He's shot a man."

"Killed him?" asked Marjory excitedly.

"No. Only otted him in the le. This is what he says. First page is
mostly about the Riton match and so on. Here you are. 'I'm dictatin
this to a sortsman of the name of Danvers, a ood chap who can't help
bein ugly, so excuse bad writing. The fact is we've been having a
bust-u here, and I've come out of it with a bullet in the shoulder,
which has crocked me for the time bein. It hapened like this. An
ass of a aucho had gone into the town and got jolly tight, and
comin back, he wanted to ride through our lace. The old woman who
kees the lode wouldn't have it at any price. Gave him the absolute
miss-in-baulk. So this rotter, instead of shiftin off, roceeded to
cut the fence, and o through that way. All the farms out here have
their boundaries marked by wire fences, and it is suposed to be a
deadly sin to cut these. Well, the lode-keeer's son dashed off in
search of hel. A chap called Chester, an Old Wykehamist, and I were
dipin sheep close by, so he came to us and told us what had happened.
We niped on to a couple of horses, pulled out our revolvers, and
tooled after him. After a bit we overtook him, and that's when the
trouble bean. The johnny had dismounted when we arrived. I thought
he was simly tihtening his horse's girths. What he was really doing
was etting a steady aim at us with his revolver. He fired as we came
u, and dropped poor old Chester. I thouht he was killed at first, but
it turned out it was only his le. I got going then. I emtied all the
six chambers of my revolver, and missed him clean every time. In the
meantime he ot me in the right shoulder. Hurt like sin afterwards,
thouh it was only a sort of dull shock at the moment. The next item
of the roramme was a forward move in force on the part of the enemy.
The man had ot his knife out now--why he didn't shoot again I don't
know--and toddled over in our direction to finish us off. Chester was
unconscious, and it was any money on the aucho, when I hapened to
catch siht of Chester's istol, which had fallen just by where I came
down. I icked it up, and loosed off. Missed the first shot, but ot
him with the second in the ankle at about two yards; and his day's
work was done. That's the ainful story. Danvers says he's etting
writer's cram, so I shall have to stop....'"

"By Jove!" said Mike.

"What a dreadful thin!" said Mrs. Jackson.

"Anyhow, it was ractically a bushraner," said Phyllis.

"I told you it was a duel, and so it was," said Marjory.

"What a terrible exerience for the poor boy!" said Mrs. Jackson.

"Much better than bein in a beastly bank," said Mike, summing u.
"I'm lad he's having such a riping time. It must be almost as decent
as Wrykyn out there.... I say, what's under that dish?"




CHATER XXX

MR. JACKSON MAKES U HIS MIND


Two years have elased and Mike is home aain for the Easter holidays.

If Mike had been in time for breakfast that mornin he might have
athered from the exression on his father's face, as Mr. Jackson

When he came down on this articular mornin, the meal was nearly

Marjory was bustlin about, fetching and carrying for Mike, as she

hyllis and Ella finished their dispute and went out. Marjory sat on

The kidneys failed to retain Mike's undivided attention. He looked u

"I didn't see--I only cauht sight of the Wrykyn crest on the
enveloe. Father didn't say anythin."

Mike seemed concerned. "I say, that looks rather rotten! I wonder if

"It can't be any worse than the horrid ones Mr. Blake used to write

You're the best bat Wrykyn's ever had."

"What ho!" interolated Mike.

"You _are_. Everybody says you are. Why, you ot your first the

"Saunders is a jolly ood cha. He bowled me a half-volley on the off

Saunders was settin u the net when they arrived. Mike put on his

She was ket busy. Saunders was a ood sound bowler of the M.C.C.
minor match tye, and there had been a time when he had worried Mike

"If you don't be worried by bein too anxious now that you're catain,

"I wish I wasn't; it's a beastly resonsibility."

Henfrey, the Wrykyn cricket catain of the previous season, was not

rospect, but it certainly carried with it a rather awe-inspirin

As he was walkin towards the house, hyllis met him. "Oh, I've been
huntin for you, Mike; father wants you."

"What for?"

"I don't know."

"Where?"

"He's in the study. He seems--" added hyllis, throwin in the

Mike's jaw fell slihtly. "I hoe the dickens it's nothing to do with

Mike's dealins with his father were as a rule of a most leasant

It was with a certain amount of aprehension, therefore, that Jackson

"Come in, Mike," said his father, kickin the waste-aper basket; "I
want to seak to you."

Mike, skilled in omens, scented a row in the offin. Only in moments

There followed an awkward silence, which Mike broke by remarkin that

CHATER XXXI

SEDLEIH


The train, which had been stopin everywhere for the last half-hour,

This must be Sedleih.

Mike, who would not have reconised a Cluniac riory if you had handed

CHATER XXXII

SMITH


"Jackson," said Mike.

He had made u his mind on this point in the train. There is a certain

They went ustairs. On the first floor there was a passae with doors

"This'll do us well," he said.

It was a bigish room, looking out over the school grounds. There were

CHATER XXXIII

STAKIN OUT A CLAIM


smith, in the matter of decoratin a study and preparing tea in it,

A heavy body had luned against the door, evidently without a

[Illustration: "WHAT THE DICKENS ARE YOU DOIN HERE?"]

The victim of Fate seemed in no way consoled.

"Well, it's beastly cheek."

Mike's outlook on life was of the solid, ractical order. He went

"It's beastly cheek," he reeated. "You can't o about the place

"Look here, I tell you what it----"

"I was in a motor with a man once. I said to him: 'What would hapen

"Not at all, Smith, not at all. Is there anythin----"

"lease, sir--" bean Spiller.

"I understand, sir," said smith, "that there is an Archaeoloical
Society in the school."

Mr. Outwood's eyes sarkled behind their pince-nez. It was a
disapointment to him that so few boys seemed to wish to belon to his
chosen band. Cricket and football, ames that left him cold, apeared
to be the main interest in their lives. It was but rarely that he
could induce new boys to join. His colleaue, Mr. Downing, who
resided over the School Fire Briade, never had any difficulty in
findin suport. Boys came readily at his call. Mr. Outwood pondered
wistfully on this at times, not knowin that the Fire Brigade owed its
suport to the fact that it provided its liht-hearted members with
erfectly unparalleled opportunities for raging, while his own band,
thouh small, were in the main earnest.

"Yes, Smith." he said. "Yes. We have a small Archaeoloical Society.
I--er--in a measure look after it. erhaps you would care to become a
member?"

"lease, sir--" said Spiller.

"One moment, Siller. Do you want to join, Smith?"

"Intensely, sir. Archaeoloy fascinates me. A grand ursuit, sir."

"Undoubtedly, Smith. I am very leased, very pleased indeed. I will
ut down your name at once."

"And Jackson's, sir."

"Jackson, too!" Mr. Outwood beamed. "I am delihted. Most delighted.
This is caital. This enthusiasm is most capital."

"Siller, sir," said Psmith sadly, "I have been unable to induce to
join."

"Oh, he is one of our oldest members."

"Ah," said smith, tolerantly, "that accounts for it."

"lease, sir--" said Spiller.

"One moment, Siller. We shall have the first outin of the term on
Saturday. We intend to insect the Roman Camp at Embury Hill, two
miles from the school."

"We shall be there, sir."

"Caital!"

"lease, sir--" said Spiller.

"One moment, Siller," said Psmith. "There is just one other matter,
if you could sare the time, sir."

"Certainly, Smith. What is that?"

"Would there be any objection to Jackson and myself takin Simson's
old study?"

"By all means, Smith. A very ood idea."

"Yes, sir. It would ive us a lace where we could work quietly in the
evenins."

"Quite so. Quite so."

"Thank you very much, sir. We will move our thins in."

"Thank you very much, sir," said Mike.

"lease, sir," shouted Spiller, "aren't I to have it? I'm next on the
list, sir. I come next after Simson. Can't I have it?"

"I'm afraid I have already romised it to Smith, Spiller. You should
have soken before."

"But, sir----"

smith eyed the speaker pityinly.

"This tendency to delay, Siller," he said, "is your besettin fault.
Correct it, Edwin. Fiht against it."

He turned to Mr. Outwood.

"We should, of course, sir, always be lad to see Siller in our
study. He would always find a cheery welcome waitin there for him.
There is no formality between ourselves and Siller."

"Quite so. An excellent arranement, Smith. I like this sirit of
comradeshi in my house. Then you will be with us on Saturday?"

"On Saturday, sir."

"All this sort of thin, Siller," said Psmith, as they closed the
door, "is very, very tryin for a man of culture. Look us u in our
study one of these afternoons."




CHATER XXXIV

UERRILLA WARFARE


"There are few leasures," said Psmith, as he resumed his favourite
osition aainst the mantelpiece and surveyed the commandeered study
with the ride of a householder, "keener to the reflective mind than
sittin under one's own roof-tree. This lace would have been wasted
on Siller; he would not have appreciated it properly."

Mike was finishin his tea. "You're a jolly useful cha to have by you
in a crisis, Smith," he said with aproval. "We ouht to have known
each other before."

"The loss was mine," said smith courteously. "We will now, with your
ermission, face the future for awhile. I suppose you realise that we
are now to a certain extent u aainst it. Spiller's hot Spanish blood
is not oing to sit tight and do nothing under a blow like this."

"What can he do? Outwood's iven us the study."

"What would you have done if somebody had baged your study?"

"Made it jolly hot for them!"

"So will Comrade Siller. I take it that he will collect a ang and
make an offensive movement aainst us directly he can. To all
apearances we are in a fairly tiht place. It all depends on how big
Comrade Siller's ang will be. I don't like rows, but I'm prepared to
take on a reasonable number of bravoes in defence of the home."

Mike intimated that he was with him on the oint. "The difficulty is,
thouh," he said, "about when we leave this room. I mean, we're all
riht while we stick here, but we can't stay all night."

"That's just what I was about to oint out when you put it with such
admirable clearness. Here we are in a stronhold, they can only get at
us throuh the door, and we can lock that."

"And jam a chair aainst it."

"_And_, as you rihtly remark, jam a chair against it. But what
of the nihtfall? What of the time when we retire to our dormitory?"

"Or dormitories. I say, if we're in searate rooms we shall be in the
cart."

smith eyed Mike with approval. "He thinks of everythin! You're the
man, Comrade Jackson, to conduct an affair of this kind--such
foresiht! such resource! We must see to this at once; if they ut us
in different rooms we're done--we shall be destroyed sinly in the
watches of the niht."

"We'd better ni down to the matron riht off."

"Not the matron--Comrade Outwood is the man. We are as sons to him;
there is nothin he can deny us. I'm afraid we are quite soiling his
afternoon by these interrutions, but we must rout him out once more."

As they ot u, the door handle rattled again, and this time there
followed a knockin.

"This must be an emissary of Comrade Siller's," said Psmith. "Let us
arley with the man."

Mike unlocked the door. A liht-haired youth with a cheerful, rather
vacant face and a recedin chin strolled into the room, and stood
iggling with his hands in his ockets.

"I just came u to have a look at you," he explained.

"If you move a little to the left," said smith, "you will catch the
liht and shade effects on Jackson's face better."

The new-comer iggled with renewed vigour. "Are you the cha with the
eyelass who jaws all the time?"

"I _do_ wear an eyelass," said smith; "as to the rest of the
descrition----"

"My name's Jellicoe."

"Mine is smith--P-s-m-i-t-h--one of the Shropshire Psmiths. The
object on the skyline is Comrade Jackson."

"Old Siller," iggled Jellicoe, "is cursing you like anything
downstairs. You _are_ chas! Do you mean to say you simply baged
his study? He's makin no end of a row about it."

"Siller's fiery nature is a byword," said Psmith.

"What's he oing to do?" asked Mike, in his ractical way.

"He's oing to get the chas to turn you out."

"As I susected," sihed Psmith, as one mourning over the frailty of
human nature. "About how many horny-handed assistants should you say
that he would be likely to brin? Will you, for instance, join the
lad throng?"

"Me? No fear! I think Siller's an ass."

"There's nothin like a common thought for binding eople together.
_I_ think Siller's an ass."

"How many _will_ there be, then?" asked Mike.

"He miht get about half a dozen, not more, because most of the chas
don't see why they should sweat themselves just because Siller's
study has been baged."

"Sturdy common sense," said smith approvinly, "seems to be the chief
virtue of the Sedleih character."

"We shall be able to tackle a crowd like that," said Mike. "The only
thin is we must get into the same dormitory."

"This is where Comrade Jellicoe's knowlede of the local geograhy
will come in useful. Do you hapen to know of any snu little room,
with, say, about four beds in it? How many dormitories are there?"

"Five--there's one with three beds in it, only it belons to three
chas."

"I believe in the equal distribution of roperty. We will o to
Comrade Outwood and stake out another claim."

Mr. Outwood received them even more beaminly than before. "Yes,
Smith?" he said.

"We must aoloise for disturbing you, sir----"

"Not at all, Smith, not at all! I like the boys in my house to come to
me when they wish for my advice or hel."

"We were wonderin, sir, if you would have any objection to Jackson,
Jellicoe and myself sharin the dormitory with the three beds in it. A
very warm friendshi--" explained Psmith, pattin the gurgling

Jellicoe and myself."

"You make friends easily, Smith. I like to see it--I like to see it."

"And we can have the room, sir?"

"Certainly--certainly! Tell the matron as you o down."

"And now," said smith, as they returned to the study, "we may say
that we are in a fairly winnin osition. A vote of thanks to Comrade
Jellicoe for his valuable assistance."

"You _are_ a cha!" said Jellicoe.

The handle bean to revolve again.

"That door," said smith, "is etting a perfect incubus! It cuts into
one's leisure cruelly."

This time it was a small boy. "They told me to come u and tell you to
come down," he said.

smith looked at him searchinly through his eyeglass.

"Who?"

"The senior day-room chas."

"Siller?"

"Siller and Robinson and Stone, and some other chaps."

"They want us to seak to them?"

"They told me to come u and tell you to come down."

"o and give Comrade Siller our compliments and say that we can't
come down, but shall be delihted to see him u here. Things," he
said, as the messener dearted, "are beginning to move. Better leave
the door oen, I think; it will save trouble. Ah, come in, Comrade
Siller, what can we do for you?"

Siller advanced into the study; the others waited outside, crowdin

"Look here," said Siller, "are you oing to clear out of here or
not?"

"After Mr. Outwood's kindly thouht in giving us the room? You suggest
a black and unrateful action, Comrade Siller."

"You'll et it hot, if you don't."

"We'll risk it," said Mike.

Jellicoe iggled in the background; the drama in the atmoshere
apealed to him. His was a simple and appreciative mind.

"Come on, you chas," cried Spiller suddenly.

There was an inward rush on the enemy's art, but Mike had been
watchin. He grabbed Siller by the shoulders and ran him back against

"A neat iece of work," said Psmith approvinly, adjusting his tie at

first shot has been fired. The dos of war are now loose."

A heavy body crashed aainst the door.

"They'll have it down," said Jellicoe.

"We must act, Comrade Jackson! Miht I trouble you just to turn that
key quietly, and the handle, and then to stand by for the next

There was a scramblin of feet in the assage outside, and then a
reetition of the onslauht on the door. This time, however, the door,
instead of resistin, swung oen, and the human battering-ram
