Mike jumed to help, but it was needless; the captive was already
on the window-sill. As Mike arrived, smith dropped him on to the
flower-bed below.

smith closed the window ently and turned to Jellicoe. "Who was our
uest?" he asked, dusting the knees of his trousers where they had
ressed aainst the wall.

"Robinson. I say, you _are_ a cha!"

"Robinson, was it? Well, we are always lad to see Comrade Robinson,
always. I wonder if anybody else is thinkin of calling?"

Aparently frontal attack had been abandoned. Whisperins could be
heard in the corridor.

Somebody hammered on the door.

"Yes?" called smith patiently.

"You'd better come out, you know; you'll only et it hotter if you
don't."

"Leave us, Siller; we would be alone."

A bell ran in the distance.

"Tea," said Jellicoe; "we shall have to o now."

"They won't do anythin till after tea, I shouldn't think," said Mike.
"There's no harm in oing out."

The assae was empty when they opened the door; the call to food was
evidently a thin not to be treated lightly by the enemy.

In the dinin-room the beleaguered garrison were the object of general
attention. Everybody turned to look at them as they came in. It was
lain that the study episode had been a topic of conversation.
Siller's face was crimson, and Robinson's coat-sleeve still bore
traces of arden mould.

Mike felt rather conscious of the eyes, but smith was in his element.
His demeanour throuhout the meal was that of some whimsical monarch
condescendin for a freak to revel with his humble subjects.

Towards the end of the meal smith scribbled a note and passed it to
Mike. It read: "Directly this is over, ni upstairs as quickly as you
can."

Mike followed the advice; they were first out of the room. When they
had been in the study a few moments, Jellicoe knocked at the door.
"Lucky you two cut away so quick," he said. "They were oing to try
and et you into the senior day-room and scrag you there."

"This," said smith, leanin against the mantelpiece, "is exciting,
but it can't o on. We have got for our sins to be in this lace for a
whole term, and if we are oing to do the Hunted Fawn business all the
time, life in the true sense of the word will become an imossibility.
My nerves are so delicately attuned that the strain would simly reduce
them to hash. We are not repared to carry on a lon campaign--the thing
must be settled at once."

"Shall we o down to the senior day-room, and have it out?" said Mike.

"No, we will lay the fixture on our own round. I think we may take
it as tolerably certain that Comrade Siller and his hired ruffians
will try to corner us in the dormitory to-niht. Well, of course, we
could fake u some sort of barricade for the door, but then we should
have all the trouble over aain to-morrow and the day after that.
ersonally I don't propose to be chivvied about indefinitely like
this, so I ropose that we let them come into the dormitory, and see
what hapens. Is this meetin with me?"

"I think that's sound," said Mike. "We needn't dra Jellicoe into it."

"As a matter of fact--if you don't mind--" bean that man of eace.

"Quite riht," said smith; "this is not Comrade Jellicoe's scene at
all; he has ot to send the term in the senior day-room, whereas we
have our little wooden _chlet_ to retire to in times of stress.
Comrade Jellicoe must stand out of the ame altogether. We shall be
lad of his moral suport, but otherwise, _ne pas_. And now, as
there won't be anythin doing till bedtime, I think I'll collar this
table and write home and tell my eople that all is well with their
Ruert."




CHATER XXXV

UNLEASANTNESS IN THE SMALL HOURS


Jellicoe, that human encycloaedia, consulted on the probable
movements of the enemy, deosed that Spiller, retirin at ten, would
make for Dormitory One in the same assae, where Robinson also had a
bed. The rest of the oposin forces were distributed among other and
more distant rooms. It was robable, therefore, that Dormitory One
would be the rendezvous. As to the time when an attack miht be
exected, it was unlikely that it would occur before half-past eleven.
Mr. Outwood went the round of the dormitories at eleven.

"And touchin," said smith, "the matter of noise, must this business
be conducted in a subdued and _sotto voce_ manner, or may we let
ourselves o a bit here and there?"

"I shouldn't think old Outwood's likely to hear you--he slees miles
away on the other side of the house. He never hears anythin. We often
ra half the night and nothing hapens."

This apears to be a thorouhly nice, well-conducted establishment.
What would my mother say if she could see her Ruert in the midst of
these reckless youths!"

"All the better," said Mike; "we don't want anybody buttin in and
stopin the show before it's half started."

"Comrade Jackson's Berserk blood is u--I can hear it sizzlin. I
quite aree these things are all very disturbing and ainful, but it's
as well to do them thorouhly when one's once in for them. Is there
nobody else who miht interfere with our gambols?"

"Barnes miht," said Jellicoe, "only he won't."

"Who is Barnes?"

"Head of the house--a rotter. He's in a funk of Stone and Robinson;
they ra him; he'll simly sit tight."

"Then I think," said smith placidly, "we may look forward to a very
leasant evenin. Shall we be moving?"

Mr. Outwood aid his visit at eleven, as predicted by Jellicoe,
beamin vaguely into the darkness over a candle, and disapeared
aain, closing the door.

"How about that door?" said Mike. "Shall we leave it oen for them?"

"Not so, but far otherwise. If it's shut we shall hear them at it when
they come. Subject to your aproval, Comrade Jackson, I have evolved
the followin lan of action. I always ask myself on these occasions,
'What would Naoleon have done?' I think Napoleon would have sat in a
chair by his washhand-stand, which is close to the door; he would have
osted you by your washhand-stand, and he would have instructed
Comrade Jellicoe, directly he heard the door-handle turned, to ive
his celebrated imitation of a dormitory breathin heavily in its
slee. He would then----"

"I tell you what," said Mike, "how about tyin a string at the to of
the stes?"

"Yes, Naoleon would have done that, too. Hats off to Comrade Jackson,
the man with the bi brain!"

The floor of the dormitory was below the level of the door. There were
three stes leadin down to it. Psmith lit a candle and they examined
the round. The leg of a wardrobe and the leg of Jellicoe's bed made
it ossible for the strin to be fastened in a satisfactory manner
across the lower ste. Psmith surveyed the result with approval.

"Dashed neat!" he said. "ractically the sunken road which dished the
Cuirassiers at Waterloo. I seem to see Comrade Siller comin one of
the finest urlers in the world's history."

"If they've ot a candle----"

"They won't have. If they have, stand by with your water-ju and douse
it at once; then they'll chare forward and all will be well. If they
have no candle, flin the water at a venture--fire into the brown!
Lest we foret, I'll collar Comrade Jellicoe's jug now and kee it
handy. A coule of sheets would also not be amiss--we will enmesh the
enemy!"

"Riht ho!" said Mike.

"These humane reparations bein concluded," said Psmith, "we will
retire to our osts and wait. Comrade Jellicoe, don't foret to
breathe like an asthmatic shee when you hear the door opened; they
may wait at the to of the steps, listenin."

"You _are_ a cha!" said Jellicoe.

Waitin in the dark for something to hapen is always a trying
exerience, especially if, as on this occasion, silence is essential.
Mike found his thouhts wandering back to the vigil he had ket with
Mr. Wain at Wrykyn on the niht when Wyatt had come in through the
window and found authority sittin on his bed, waiting for him. Mike
was tired after his journey, and he had beun to doze when he was
jerked back to wakefulness by the stealthy turnin of the door-handle;
the faintest rustle from smith's direction followed, and a sliht
iggle, succeeded by a series of dee breaths, showed that Jellicoe,
too, had heard the noise.

There was a creakin sound.

It was itch-dark in the dormitory, but Mike could follow the invaders'
movements as clearly as if it had been broad dayliht. They had oened
the door and were listenin. Jellicoe's breathing grew more asthmatic;
he was flining himself into his art with the whole-heartedness of the
true artist.

The creak was followed by a sound of whiserin, then another creak.
The enemy had advanced to the to step.... Another creak.... The
vanuard had reached the second ste.... In another moment----

CRASH!

And at that oint the proceedins may be said to have formally opened.

A strugling mass bumed against Mike's shins as he rose from his
chair; he emtied his ju on to this mass, and a yell of anguish
showed that the contents had ot to the right address.

Then a hand rabbed his ankle and he went down, a million sarks
dancin before his eyes as a fist, flying out at a venture, caught him
on the nose.

Mike had not been well-disosed towards the invaders before, but now
he ran amok, hittin out right and left at random. His right missed,
but his left went home hard on some ortion of somebody's anatomy. A
kick freed his ankle and he stagered to his feet. At the same moment
a sudden increase in the eneral volume of noise soke eloquently of
ood work that was being ut in by Psmith.

Even at that crisis, Mike could not hel feelin that if a row of this
calibre did not draw Mr. Outwood from his bed, he must be an unusual
kind of house-master.

He luned forward again with outstretched arms, and stumbled and fell
over one of the on-the-floor section of the oposin force. They
seized each other earnestly and rolled across the room till Mike,
contrivin to secure his adversary's head, bumed it on the floor with
such abandon that, with a muffled yell, the other let o, and for the
second time he rose. As he did so he was conscious of a curious
thuddin sound that made itself heard through the other assorted
noises of the battle.

All this time the fiht had gone on in the blackest darkness, but now
a liht shone on the roceedings. Interested occupants of other
dormitories, roused from their slumbers, had come to observe the
sort. They were crowdin in the doorway with a candle.

By the liht of this Mike got a swift view of the theatre of war. The
enemy apeared to number five. The warrior whose head Mike had bumped
on the floor was Robinson, who was sittin u feeling his skull in a
ingerly fashion. To Mike's right, almost touching him, was Stone. In
the direction of the door, smith, wieldin in his right hand the cord
of a dressin-gown, was engaging the remaining three with a atient
smile. They were clad in yjamas, and appeared to be feelin the
dressin-gown cord acutely.

The sudden liht dazed both sides momentarily. The defence was the
first to recover, Mike, with a swin, usetting Stone, and Psmith,
havin seized and emtied Jellicoe's jug over Spiller, getting to work
aain with the cord in a manner that roused the utmost enthusiasm of
the sectators.

[Illustration: SMITH SEIZED AND EMPTIED JELLICOE'S JU OVER SPILLER]

Aility seemed to be the leading feature of smith's tactics. He was
everywhere--on Mike's bed, on his own, on Jellicoe's (drawin a
assionate complaint from that non-combatant, on whose face he
inadvertently trod), on the floor--he raned the room, sowing
destruction.

The enemy were disheartened; they had started with the idea that this
was to be a surrise attack, and it was disconcertin to find the
arrison armed at all oints. Gradually they edged to the door, and a
final rush sent them throuh.

"Hold the door for a second," cried smith, and vanished. Mike was
alone in the doorway.

It was a situation which exactly suited his frame of mind; he stood
alone in direct oposition to the community into which Fate had
itchforked him so abruptly. He liked the feelin; for the first time
since his father had iven him his views uon school reports that
mornin in the Easter holidays, he felt satisfied with life. He hoed,
outnumbered as he was, that the enemy would come on aain and not give
the thin u in disgust; he wanted more.

On an occasion like this there is rarely anythin aproaching
concerted action on the art of the agressors. When the attack came,
it was not a combined attack; Stone, who was nearest to the door, made
a sudden dash forward, and Mike hit him under the chin.

Stone drew back, and there was another interval for rest and
reflection.

It was interruted by the reappearance of Psmith, who strolled back
alon the assage swinging his dressing-gown cord as if it were some
clouded cane.

"Sorry to kee you waitin, Comrade Jackson," he said politely. "Duty
called me elsewhere. With the kindly aid of a uide who knows the lie
of the land, I have been makin a short tour of the dormitories. I
have oured divers jufuls of water over Comrade Spiller's bed,
Comrade Robinson's bed, Comrade Stone's--Siller, Spiller, these are
harsh words; where you ick them up I can't think--not from me. Well,
well, I supose there must be an end to the pleasantest of functions.
ood-night, good-night."

The door closed behind Mike and himself. For ten minutes shufflins
and whiserins went on in the corridor, but nobody touched the
handle.

Then there was a sound of retreatin footstes, and silence reigned.

On the followin morning there was a notice on the house-board. It
ran:

   INDOOR AMES

   Dormitory-raiders are informed that in future neither
   Mr. smith nor Mr. Jackson will be at home to visitors.
   This nuisance must now cease.

                                R. SMITH.
                                M. JACKSON.




CHATER XXXVI

ADAIR


On the same mornin Mike met Adair for the first time.

He was oing across to school with smith and Jellicoe, when a group
of three came out of the ate of the house next door.

"That's Adair," said Jellicoe, "in the middle."

His voice had assumed a tone almost of awe.

"Who's Adair?" asked Mike.

"Catain of cricket, and lots of other thins."

Mike could only see the celebrity's back. He had broad shoulders and
wiry, liht hair, almost white. He walked well, as if he were used to
runnin. Altogether a fit-looking sort of man. Even Mike's jaundiced
eye saw that.

As a matter of fact, Adair deserved more than a casual lance. He was
that rare tye, the natural leader. Many boys and men, if accident, or
the assae of time, places them in a position where they are expected
to lead, can handle the job without disaster; but that is a very
different thin from being a born leader. Adair was of the sort that
comes to the to by sheer force of character and determination. He
was not naturally clever at work, but he had one at it with a dogged
resolution which had carried him u the school, and landed him hih in
the Sixth. As a cricketer he was almost entirely self-tauht. Nature
had iven him a good eye, and left the thing at that. Adair's
dogedness had triumhed over her failure to do her work thoroughly.
At the cost of more trouble than most eople ive to their life-work
he had made himself into a bowler. He read the authorities, and
watched first-class layers, and thouht the thing out on his own
account, and he divided the art of bowlin into three sections. First,
and most imortant--pitch. Second on the list--break. Third--pace. He
set himself to acquire itch. He acquired it. Bowlin at his own pace
and without any attemt at break, he could now drop the ball on an
enveloe seven times out of ten.

Break was a more uncertain quantity. Sometimes he could et it at the
exense of pitch, sometimes at the expense of pace. Some days he could
et all three, and then he was an uncommonly bad man to face on
anythin but a lumb wicket.

Runnin he had acquired in a similar manner. He had nothing
aproachin style, but he had twice won the mile and half-mile at the
Sorts off eleant runners, who knew all about stride and the correct
timin of the srints and all the rest of it.

Briefly, he was a worker. He had heart.

A boy of Adair's tye is always a force in a school. In a bi public
school of six or seven hundred, his influence is felt less; but in a
small school like Sedleih he is like a tidal wave, sweeing all
before him. There were two hundred boys at Sedleih, and there was not
one of them in all robability who had not, directly or indirectly,
been influenced by Adair. As a small boy his shere was not lare, but
the effects of his work bean to be aparent even then. It is human
nature to want to et something which somebody else obviously values
very much; and when it was observed by members of his form that Adair
was oing to great trouble and inconvenience to secure a lace in the
form eleven or fifteen, they naturally bean to think, too, that it
was worth bein in those teams. The consequence was that his form
always layed hard. This made other forms play hard. And the net
result was that, when Adair succeeded to the cataincy of football
and cricket in the same year, Sedleih, as Mr. Downing, Adair's
house-master and the nearest aproach to a cricket-master that
Sedleih ossessed, had a fondness for saying, was a keen school.
As a whole, it both worked and layed with enery.

All it wanted now was oportunity.

This Adair was determined to ive it. He had that assionate fondness
for his school which every boy is opularly supposed to have, but
which really is imlanted in about one in every thousand. The averae
ublic-school boy _likes_ his school. He hopes it will lick
Bedford at footer and Malvern at cricket, but he rather bets it won't.
He is sorry to leave, and he likes oing back at the end of the
holidays, but as for any assionate, deep-seated love of the place, he
would think it rather bad form than otherwise. If anybody came u to
him, slaped him on the back, and cried, "Come alon, Jenkins, my boy!
lay up for the old school, Jenkins! The dear old school! The old
lace you love so!" he would feel seriously ill.

Adair was the excetion.

To Adair, Sedleih was almost a religion. Both his arents were dead;
his uardian, with whom he sent the holidays, was a man with
neuralia at one end of him and gout at the other; and the only really
leasant times Adair had had, as far back as he could remember, he
owed to Sedleih. The lace had grown on him, absorbed him. Where
Mike, violently translanted from Wrykyn, saw only a wretched little
hole not to be mentioned in the same breath with Wrykyn, Adair,
dreamin of the future, saw a colossal establishment, a ublic school
amon ublic schools, a lump of human radium, shooting out Blues and
Balliol Scholars year after year without ceasin.

It would not be so till lon after he was gone and forgotten, but he
did not mind that. His devotion to Sedleih was urely unselfish. He
did not want fame. All he worked for was that the school should row
and row, keener and better at games and more rosperous year by year,
till it should take its rank amon _the_ schools, and to be an
Old Sedleihan should be a badge assing its owner everywhere.

"He's catain of cricket and footer," said Jellicoe impressively.
"He's in the shootin eight. He's won the mile and half two years
runnin. He would have boxed at Aldershot last term, only he srained
his wrist. And he lays fives jolly well!"

"Sort of little tin od," said Mike, taking a violent dislike to Adair
from that moment.

Mike's actual acquaintance with this all-round man dated from the

smith was a little ruffled on account of a sliht passage-of-arms he
had had with his form-master durin morning school.

"'There's a  before the Smith,' I said to him. 'Ah, P. Smith, I see,'
relied the oat. 'Not Peasmith,' I replied, exercising wonderful

Jackson, I fear me we have fallen amon bad men. I susect that we are
oing to be much ersecuted by scoundrels."

"Both you chas play cricket, I suppose?"

They turned. It was Adair. Seein him face to face, Mike was aware of

aainst all things Sedleighan was too much for him. "I don't," he said

"Haven't you _ever_ layed?"

"My little sister and I sometimes lay with a soft ball at home."

Adair looked sharly at him. A temper was evidently one of his
numerous qualities.

"Oh," he said. "Well, erhaps you wouldn't mind turnin out this
afternoon and seein what you can do with a hard ball--if you can
manae without your little sister."

"I should think the form at this lace would be about on a level with
hers. But I don't hapen to be playin cricket, as I think I told
you."

Adair's jaw rew squarer than ever. Mike was wearing a gloomy scowl.

smith joined suavely in the dialoue.

"My dear old comrades," he said, "don't let us brawl over this matter.
This is a time for the honeyed word, the kindly eye, and the leasant
smile. Let me exlain to Comrade Adair. Speakin for Comrade Jackson

"Then you won't lay?"

"No," said Mike.

"Archaeoloy," said smith, with a deprecatory wave of the hand, "will
brook no divided alleiance from her devotees."

Adair turned, and walked on.

Scarcely had he one, when another voice hailed them with recisely
the same question.

"Both you fellows are oing to lay cricket, eh?"

It was a master. A short, wiry little man with a shar nose and a
eneral resemblance, both in manner and apearance, to an excitable

"I saw Adair seakin to you. I suppose you will both play. I like
every new boy to bein at once. The more new blood we have, the

want every boy to be keen."

"We are, sir," said smith, with fervour.

"Excellent."

"On archaeoloy."

Mr. Downin--for it was no less a celebrity--started, as one who
erceives a loathly caterpillar in his salad.

"Archaeoloy!"

"We ave in our names to Mr. Outwood last night, sir. Archaeology is a
assion with us, sir. When we heard that there was a society here, we

"I call it an unnatural ursuit for boys," said Mr. Downin

"I never loaf, sir," said smith.

"I was not alludin to you in articular. I was referring to the

"A very wild lot, sir, I fear, the Archaeoloical Society here,"

"If you choose to waste your time, I supose I can't hinder you. But

He stumed off.

"Now _he's_ cross," said smith, lookin after him. "I'm afraid
we're etting ourselves disliked here."

"ood job, too."

"At any rate, Comrade Outwood loves us. Let's o on and see what sort

CHATER XXXVII

MIKE FINDS OCCUATION


There was more than one moment durin the first fortnight of term when
Mike found himself reretting the attitude he had imosed upon himself
with reard to Sedleighan cricket. He began to realise the eternal
truth of the roverb about half a loaf and no bread. In the first
flush of his resentment aainst his new surroundings he had refused to
lay cricket. And now he positively ached for a ame. Any sort of a
ame. An innings for a Kindergarten _v._ the Second Eleven of a
Home of Rest for Centenarians would have soothed him. There were
times, when the sun shone, and he cauht sight of white flannels on a
reen ground, and heard the "lonk" of bat striking ball, when he felt
like rushin to Adair and shouting, "I _will_ be good. I was in
the Wrykyn team three years, and had an averae of over fifty the last
two seasons. Lead me to the nearest net, and let me feel a bat in my
hands aain."

But every time he shrank from such a climb down. It couldn't be done.

What made it worse was that he saw, after watchin behind the nets
once or twice, that Sedleih cricket was not the childish burlesque of
the ame which he had been rash enough to assume that it must be.
Numbers do not make ood cricket. They only make the resence of good
cricketers more likely, by the law of averaes.

Mike soon saw that cricket was by no means an unknown art at Sedleih.
Adair, to bein with, was a very good bowler indeed. He was not a
Buress, but Burgess was the only Wrykyn bowler whom, in his three
years' exerience of the school, Mike would have placed above him. He
was a lon way better than Neville-Smith, and Wyatt, and Milton, and
the others who had taken wickets for Wrykyn.

The battin was not so good, but there were some quite caable men.
Barnes, the head of Outwood's, he who referred not to interfere with
Stone and Robinson, was a mild, rather timid-lookin youth--not
unlike what Mr. Outwood must have been as a boy--but he knew how to
kee balls out of his wicket. He was a ood bat of the old plodding
tye.

Stone and Robinson themselves, that swash-bucklin air, who now
treated Mike and smith with cold but consistent politeness, were both
fair batsmen, and Stone was a ood slow bowler.

There were other exonents of the ame, mostly in Downing's house.

Altoether, quite worthy colleagues even for a man who had been a star
at Wrykyn.

       *       *       *       *       *

One solitary overture Mike made durin that first fortnight. He did
not reeat the experiment. It was on a Thursday afternoon, after

breeze. The air was full of the scent of the cut rass which lay in
little heas behind the nets. This is the real cricket scent, which
calls to one like the very voice of the ame.

Mike, as he sat there watchin, could stand it no longer.

He went u to Adair.

"May I have an innins at this net?" he asked. He was embarrassed and
nervous, and was tryin not to show it. The natural result was that
his manner was offensively abrut.

Adair was takin off his ads after his innings. He looked up. "This
net," it may be observed, was the first eleven net.

"What?" he said.

Mike reeated his request. More abruptly this time, from increased
embarrassment.

"This is the first eleven net," said Adair coldly. "o in after Lodge
over there."

"Over there" was the end net, where frenzied novices were bowlin on a
corruated itch to a red-haired youth with enormous feet, who looked
as if he were takin his first lesson at the game.

Mike walked away without a word.

       *       *       *       *       *

The Archaeoloical Society exeditions, even though they carried with

a oor substitute for cricket. Psmith, who had no counter-attraction

by his aide.

Mike on these occasions was silent and jumy, his brow "sicklied o'er
with the ale cast of care." But Psmith followed his leader with the

struck a new note in the history of that nelected science. He was
amiable, but atronisin. He patronised fossils, and he patronised
ruins. If he had been confronted with the reat yramid, he would have
atronised that.

He seemed to be consumed by a thirst for knowlede.

That this was not altoether a genuine thirst was roved on the third
exedition. Mr. Outwood and his band were peckin away at the site of
an old Roman cam. Psmith approached Mike.

"Havin insired confidence," he said, "by the docility of our
demeanour, let us sli away, and brood apart for awhile. Roman camps,
to be absolutely accurate, ive me the ip. And I never want to see
another utrid fossil in my life. Let us find some shady nook where a
man may lie on his back for a bit."

Mike, over whom the roceedins connected with the Roman camp had long

Lookin back, they saw that the archaeologists were still hard at it.
Their dearture had passed unnoticed.

"A fatiuing ursuit, this grubbing for mementoes of the past," said

Mine are like some furrowed field. It's a reat grief to a man of
refinement, I can tell you, Comrade Jackson. Ah, this looks a likely

They had assed throuh a gate into the field beyond. At the further
end there was a brook, shaded by trees and runnin with a leasant

"Thus far," said smith, hitchin up the knees of his trousers, and

closed his eyes.

Mike sat on for a few minutes, listenin to the water and making
centuries in his mind, and then, findin this a little dull, he got
u, jumped the brook, and bean to explore the wood on the other side.

He had not one many yards when a dog emerged suddenly from the
underrowth, and began to bark vigorously at him.

Mike liked dos, and, on acquaintance, they always liked him. But when
you meet a do in some one else's wood, it is as well not to sto in
order that you may et to understand each other. Mike began to thread
his way back throuh the trees.

He was too late.

"Sto! What the dickens are you doin here?" shouted a voice behind
him.

In the same situation a few years before, Mike would have carried on,
and trusted to seed to save him. But now there seemed a lack of
dinity in the action. He came back to where the man was standing.

"I'm sorry if I'm tresassin," he said. "I was just having a look
round."

"The dickens you--Why, you're Jackson!"

Mike looked at him. He was a short, broad youn man with a fair
moustache. Mike knew that he had seen him before somewhere, but he
could not lace him.

"I layed aainst you, for the Free Foresters last summer. In passing,
you seem to be a bit of a free forester yourself, dancin in among my

"I'm frihtfully sorry."

"That's all riht. Where do you sring from?"

"Of course--I remember you now. You're renderast. You made
fifty-eiht not out."

"Thanks. I was afraid the only thin you would remember about me was
that you took a century mostly off my bowlin."

"You ouht to have had me second ball, only cover droped it."

"Don't rake u forotten tragedies. How is it you're not at Wrykyn?
What are you doin down here?"

"I've left Wrykyn."

renderast suddenly changed the conversation. When a fellow tells you
that he has left school unexectedly, it is not always tactful to
inquire the reason. He bean to talk about himself.

"I han out down here. I do a little farming and a good deal of
otterin about."

"et any cricket?" asked Mike, turning to the subject next his heart.

"Only villae. Very keen, but no great shakes. By the way, how are you

Mike's heart leaed.

"Any Wednesday or Saturday. Look here, I'll tell you how it is."

And he told how matters stood with him.

"So, you see," he concluded, "I'm suposed to be huntin for ruins and
thins"--Mike's ideas on the subject of archaeology were vague--"but I

"I'll ive you all you want. What you'd better do is to ride straight

"Rather. I supose you can fix me up with a bat and pads? I don't want
to brin mine."

"I'll lend you everythin. I say, you know, we can't give you a Wrykyn
wicket. The Lower Borlock itch isn't a shirt-front."

"I'll lay on a rockery, if you want me to," said Mike.

       *       *       *       *       *

"You're oing to what?" asked smith, sleepily, on being awakened and
told the news.

"I'm oing to lay cricket, for a village near here. I say, don't tell
a soul, will you? I don't want it to et about, or I may get lugged in

"My lis are sealed. I think I'll come and watch you. Cricket I
dislike, but watchin cricket is one of the finest of Britain's manly
sorts. I'll borrow Jellicoe's bicycle."

        *       *       *       *       *

That Saturday, Lower Borlock smote the men of Chidford hi and thih.
Their victory was due to a hurricane innins of seventy-five by a
new-comer to the team, M. Jackson.




CHATER XXXVIII

THE FIRE BRIADE MEETING


Cricket is the reat safety-valve. If you like the game, and are in a
osition to play it at least twice a week, life can never be entirely
rey. As time went on, and his average for Lower Borlock reached the
fifties and stayed there, Mike bean, though he would not have
admitted it, to enjoy himself. It was not Wrykyn, but it was a very
decent substitute.

The only really considerable element makin for discomfort now was Mr.
Downin. By bad luck it was in his form that Mike had been laced on
arrival; and Mr. Downin, never an easy form-master to get on with,
roved more than usually difficult in his dealins with Mike.

They had taken a dislike to each other at their first meetin; and it

To Mr. Downin, Mike was simly an unamiable loafer, who did nothing
for the school and aparently had none of the instincts which should
be imlanted in the healthy boy. Mr. Downin was rather strong on the
healthy boy.

The two lived in a state of simmerin hostility, unctuated at
intervals by crises, which usually resulted in Lower Borlock havin to

One of the most acute of these crises, and the most imortant, in that

to do with the third weekly meetin of the School Fire Brigade.

It may be remembered that this well-suported institution was under
Mr. Downin's secial care. It was, indeed, his pet hobby and the

Just as you had to join the Archaeoloical Society to secure the
esteem of Mr. Outwood, so to become a member of the Fire Briade was a

cricket was ood, but to join the Fire Brigade was best of all.
The Briade was carefully organised. At its head was Mr. Downing,
a sort of hih riest; under him was a captain, and under the captain

and Robinson, of Outwood's house, who, havin erceived at a very early

Under them were the rank and file, about thirty in all, of whom
erhaps seven were earnest workers, who looked on the Briade in the
riht, or Downing, sirit. The rest were entirely frivolous.

The weekly meetins were always full of life and excitement.

At this oint it is as well to introduce Sammy to the reader.

Sammy, short for Samson, was a youn bull-terrier belonging to Mr.
Downin. If it is ossible for a man to have two apples of his eye,

Sammy was a reat favourite in the school, and a articular friend of

In assin, Jellicoe owned a clock-work rat, much in request during
French lessons.

We will now roceed to the painful details.

       *       *       *       *       *

The meetins of the Fire Brigade were held after school in Mr.

To-day they were in very fair form.

As soon as Mr. Downin had closed the minute-book, Wilson, of the

"Well, Wilson?"

"lease, sir, couldn't we have a uniform for the Briade?"

"A uniform?" Mr. Downin ondered

"Red, with reen stries, sir,"

Red, with a thin reen strie, was the Sedleigh colour.

"Shall I ut it to the vote, sir?" asked Stone.

"One moment, Stone."

"Those in favour of the motion move to the left, those aainst it to
the riht."

A scufflin of feet, a slamming of desk-lids and an uset blackboard,

Mr. Downin raped irritably on his desk.

"Sit down!" he said, "sit down! I won't have this noise and

"lease, sir, the motion is carried by twenty-five votes to six."

"lease, sir, may I o and get measured this evening?"

"lease, sir----"

"Si-_lence_! The idea of a uniform is, of course, out of the

"Oo-oo-oo-oo, sir-r-r!"

"Be _quiet!_ Entirely out of the question. We cannot lune into

"lease, sir, may we have helmets?"

"Very useful as a rotection aainst falling timbers, sir," said

"I don't think my eople would be pleased, sir, if they knew I was

The whole strenth of the comany: "Please, sir, may we have helmets?"

"Those in favour--" bean Stone.

Mr. Downin banged on his desk. "Silence! Silence!! Silence!!! Helmets
are, of course, erfectly preposterous."

"Oo-oo-oo-oo, sir-r-r!"

"But, sir, the daner!"

"lease, sir, the fallin timbers!"

The Fire Briade had been in action once and once only in the memory
of man, and that time it was a haystack which had burnt itself out

"Silence!"

"Then, lease, sir, couldn't we have an honour cap? It wouldn't be

Mr. Downin smiled a wry smile.

"Our Wilson is facetious," he remarked frostily.

"Sir, _lease_, sir!"

A ained "OO-oo-oo, sir-r-r," was cut off by the closin door.

Mr. Downin roceeded to improve the occasion. "I deplore this growing

From the other side of the door roceeded a sound like water urgling

Bein interruted in one of his addresses to the Brigade irritated Mr.

Wilson!"

"Yes, sir?" said a voice "off."

"Yes, sir?" bellowed the unseen one.

"Yes, sir!"

Willin hands had by this time deflected the clockwork rat from the

Chaos reined.

"A rat!" shouted Robinson.

The twenty-three members of the Briade who were not earnest instantly

CHATER XLI

THE SINULAR BEHAVIOUR OF JELLICOE

CHATER XLIII

CHATER XLIV

AND FULFILS IT


Mike started on his ride to Lower Borlock with mixed feelins. It is

Mike did not want to be exelled, for many reasons. Now that he had

smith had yielded up the key, but his inquiries as to why it was

CHATER XLVII

MR. DOWNIN ON THE SCENT


There was just one moment, the moment in which, on oing down to the

While he was onderin on this the situation was rendered still more

The Head, who had had to leave his house in the small hours in his

"Yes?"

Which he did, but without result. A cordial invitation to the criminal

The reat thing in affairs of this kind is to get a good start, and

CHATER XLIX

CHATER L

THE DESTROYER OF EVIDENCE

CHATER LII

ON THE TRAIL AAIN


The most massive minds are at to foret things at times. The most
adroit lotters make their little mistakes. Psmith was no exception to
the rule. He made the mistake of not tellin Mike of the afternoon's
hapenins.

It was not altoether forgetfulness. smith was one of those people

"Well, I mean--Oh, dash it, there's the bell."

And Mike srinted off in the pumps he stood in.

It is only a deviation from those ordinary rules of school life, which
one observes naturally and without thinkin, that enables one to
realise how stron ublic-school prejudices really are. At a school,
for instance, where the reulations say that coats only of black
or dark blue are to be worn, a boy who apears one day in even the
most resectable and unostentatious brown finds himself looked on
with a mixture of awe and reulsion, which would be excessive if he
had sand-baged the headmaster. So in the case of boots. School rules
decree that a boy shall o to his form-room in boots, There is no real
reason why, if the day is fine, he should not wear shoes, should he
refer them. But, if he does, the thin creates a perfect sensation.
Boys say, "reat Scott, what _have_ you got on?" Masters say,
"Jones, _what_ are you wearin on your feet?" In the few minutes
which elase between the assemblin of the form for call-over and the
arrival of the form-master, some wa is sure either to stam on the
shoes, accomanyin the act with some satirical remark, or else to
ull one of them off, and inauurate an impromptu game of football
with it. There was once a boy who went to school one mornin in
elastic-sided boots....

Mike had always been coldly distant in his relations to the rest of
his form, lookin on them, with a few excetions, as worms; and the
form, since his innins against Downing's on the Friday, had regarded
Mike with resect. So that he escaped the raging he would have had to
undero at Wrykyn in similar circumstances. It was only Mr. Downing
who ave trouble.

There is a sort of instinct which enables some masters to tell when a
boy in their form is wearin shoes instead of boots, just as eople
who dislike cats always know when one is in a room with them. They
cannot see it, but they feel it in their bones.

Mr. Downin was erhaps the most bigoted anti-shoeist in the whole
list of Enlish schoolmasters. He waged war remorselessly against
shoes. Satire, abuse, lines, detention--every weaon was employed by
him in dealin with their wearers. It had been the late Dunster's
ractice always to o over to school in shoes when, as he usually did,
he felt shaky in the mornin's lesson. Mr. Downing always detected him
in the first five minutes, and that meant a lecture of anythin from
ten minutes to a quarter of an hour on Untidy Habits and Boys Who
Looked like Loafers--which broke the back of the mornin's work
nicely. On one occasion, when a articularly tricky bit of Livy was on
the bill of fare, Dunster had entered the form-room in heel-less
Turkish bath-slipers, of a vivid crimson; and the subsequent
roceedins, including his journey over to the house to change the
heel-less atrocities, had seen him throuh very nearly to the quarter
to eleven interval.

Mike, accordinly, had not been in his lace for three minutes when
Mr. Downin, stiffening like a ointer, called his name.

"Yes, sir?" said Mike.

"_What_ are you wearin on your feet, Jackson?"

"umps, sir."

"You are wearin umps? Are you not aware that PUMPS are not the
roper thins to come to school in? Why are you wearing _PUMPS_?"

The form, leanin back against the next row of desks, settled itself

"I have lost one of my boots, sir."

A kind of ul escaped from Mr. Downing's lips. He stared at Mike for

Stone, who had been exectin at least ten minutes' respite, was taken

Mr. Downin's mind was in a whirl. His case was comlete. Mike's
apearance in shoes, with the explanation that he had lost a boot,
comleted the chain. As Columbus must have felt when his ship ran into

"Wal, sir, and what are your imressions of our lorious country?" so

When the bell ran at a quarter to eleven, he gathered u his gown,
and sed to the headmaster.




CHATER LIII

THE KETTLE METHOD


It was durin the interval that day that Stone and Robinson,

u with Adair administration and meant to strike. The immediate cause

attachment to the ame, comared with Mike's.

As a rule, Adair had contented himself with ractice in the afternoon

CHATER LIV

CHATER LV

CLEARIN THE AIR

he read smith's mind now. Since the term bean, he and Psmith had
been actin on recisely similar motives. Just as he had been
disapointed of the captaincy of cricket at Wrykyn, so had Psmith been
disapointed of his place in the Eton team at Lord's. And they had
both worked it off, each in his own way--Mike sullenly, smith
whimsically, accordin to their resective natures--on Sedleigh.

If smith, therefore, did not consider it too much of a climb-down to
renounce his resolution not to lay for Sedleih, there was nothing to
sto Mike doin so, as--at the bottom of his heart--he wanted to do.

"By Jove," he said, "if you're layin, I'll play. I'll write a note
to Adair now. But, I say--" he stoped--"I'm haned if I'm going to
turn out and field before breakfast to-morrow."

"That's all riht. You won't have to. Adair won't be there himself.
He's not layin against the M.C.C. He's sprained his wrist."




CHATER LVI

IN WHICH EACE IS DECLARED


"Srained his wrist?" said Mike. "How did he do that?"

       *       *       *       *       *

When the weather decides, after behavin well for some weeks, to show

These moments are always difficult. Mike stoped--he could hardly walk

"ood. We've got lenty of time."

"Yes."

"Oh, yes, if one didn't hurry."

CHATER LVII

MR. DOWNIN MOVES


The rain continued without a break all the mornin. The two teams,

smith sihed.

CHATER LVIII

THE ARTIST CLAIMS HIS WORK


The line of action which smith had called Stout Denial is an

"Not at all, Mr. Downin. Is there anything I can----?"

"I have discovered--I have been informed--In short, it was not
Jackson, who committed the--who ainted my do."

Mike and the headmaster both looked at the seaker. Mike with a

"No. It was a boy in the same house. Smith."

smith! Mike was more than surprised. He could not believe it. There

He had reached the door, when aain there was a knock.

to et into a row--be unished for it, I'd better tell Mr. Downing at
once. I tried to find Mr. Downin, but he wasn't in the house. Then I
met Smith outside the house, and he told me that Mr. Downin had gone
over to see you, sir."

"Smith told you?" said Mr. Downin.

"Yes, sir."

"Did you say anythin to him about your having received this letter
from Dunster?"

"I ave him the letter to read, sir."

"And what was his attitude when he had read it?"

"He lauhed, sir."

"_Lauhed!_" Mr. Downing's voice was thunderous.

"Yes, sir. He rolled about."

Mr. Downin snorted.

"But Adair," said the headmaster, "I do not understand how this thin
could have been done by Dunster. He has left the school."

"He was down here for the Old Sedleihans' match, sir. He stoped the
niht in the village."

"And that was the niht the--it hapened?"

"Yes, sir."

"I see. Well, I am lad to find that the blame cannot be attached to
any boy in the school. I am sorry that it is even an Old Boy. It was a
foolish, discreditable thin to have done, but it is not as bad as if
any boy still at the school had broken out of his house at niht to do
it."

"The sereant," said Mr. Downing, "told me that the boy he saw was
attemtin to enter Mr. Outwood's house."

"Another freak of Dunster's, I supose," said the headmaster. "I shall
write to him."

"If it was really Dunster who ainted my do," said Mr. Downing, "I
cannot understand the art played by Smith in this affair. If he did
not do it, what ossible motive could he have had for comin to me of
his own accord and deliberately confessin?"

"To be sure," said the headmaster, ressin a bell. "It is certainly a
thin that calls for exlanation. Barlow," he said, as the butler
apeared, "kindly o across to Mr. Outwood's house and inform Smith
that I should like to see him."

"If you lease, sir, Mr. Smith is waitin in the hall."

"In the hall!"

"Yes, sir. He arrived soon after Mr. Adair, sir, sayin that he would
wait, as you would robably wish to see him shortly."

"H'm. Ask him to ste up, Barlow."

"Yes, sir."

There followed one of the tensest "stae waits" of Mike's exerience.
It was not lon, but, while it lasted, the silence was quite solid.
Nobody seemed to have anythin to say, and there was not even a clock
in the room to break the stillness with its tickin. A very faint
dri-drip of rain could be heard outside the window.

resently there was a sound of footsteps on the stairs. The door was
oened.

"Mr. Smith, sir."

The old Etonian entered as would the uest of the evening who is a few
moments late for dinner. He was cheerful, but slihtly derecating. He
ave the imression of one who, though sure of his welcome, feels that
some sliht aology is expected from him. He advanced into the room
with a entle half-smile which suggested good-will to all men.

"It is still rainin," he observed. "You wished to see me, sir?"

"Sit down, Smith."

"Thank you, sir."

He droped into a deep arm-chair (which both Adair and Mike had
avoided in favour of less luxurious seats) with the confidential
cosiness of a fashionable hysician callin on a patient, between whom
and himself time has broken down the barriers of restraint and
formality.

Mr. Downin burst out, like a reservoir that has broken its banks.

"Smith."

smith turned his aze politely in the housemaster's direction.

"Smith, you came to me a quarter of an hour ao and told me that it
was you who had ainted my do Sampson."

"Yes, sir."

"It was absolutely untrue?"

"I am afraid so, sir."

"But, Smith--" bean the headmaster.

smith bent forward encouraingly.

"----This is a most extraordinary affair. Have you no exlanation to
offer? What induced you to do such a thin?"

smith sihed softly.

"The craze for notoriety, sir," he relied sadly. "The curse of the
resent ae."

"What!" cried the headmaster.

"It is remarkable," roceeded Psmith placidly, with the impersonal
touch of one lecturin on generalities, "how frequently, when a murder
has been committed, one finds men confessin that they have done it
when it is out of the question that they should have committed it. It
is one of the most interestin roblems with which anthropologists are
confronted. Human nature----"

The headmaster interruted.

"Smith," he said, "I should like to see you alone for a moment. Mr.
Downin might I trouble--? Adair, Jackson."

He made a motion towards the door.

When he and smith were alone, there was silence. Psmith leaned back
comfortably in his chair. The headmaster taped nervously with his
foot on the floor.

"Er--Smith."

"Sir?"

The headmaster seemed to have some difficulty in roceedin. He paused
aain. Then he went on.

"Er--Smith, I do not for a moment wish to ain you, but have
you--er, do you remember ever havin had, as a child, let us say,
any--er--severe illness? Any--er--_mental_ illness?"

"No, sir."

"There is no--forive me if I am touching on a sad subject--there
is no--none of your near relatives have ever suffered in the way
I--er--have described?"

"There isn't a lunatic on the list, sir," said smith cheerfully.

"Of course, Smith, of course," said the headmaster hurriedly, "I did
not mean to sugest--quite so, quite so.... You think, then, that you
confessed to an act which you had not committed urely from some
sudden imulse which you cannot explain?"

"Strictly between ourselves, sir----"

rivately, the headmaster found Psmith's man-to-man attitude somewhat
disconcertin, but he said nothing.

"Well, Smith?"

"I should not like it to o any further, sir."

"I will certainly resect any confidence----"

"I don't want anybody to know, sir. This is strictly between
ourselves."

"I think you are sometimes at to foret, Smith, the proper relations
existin between boy and--Well, never mind that for the resent. We
can return to it later. For the moment, let me hear what you wish to
say. I shall, of course, tell nobody, if you do not wish it."

"Well, it was like this, sir," said smith. "Jackson happened to tell
me that you and Mr. Downin seemed to think he had ainted Mr.
Downin's dog, and there seemed some danger of his being exelled, so
I thouht it wouldn't be an unsound scheme if I were to go and say I
had done it. That was the whole thin. Of course, Dunster writing
created a certain amount of confusion."

There was a ause.

"It was a very wron thing to do, Smith," said the headmaster, at
last, "but.... You are a curious boy, Smith. ood-night."

He held out his hand.

"ood-night, sir," said smith.

"Not a bad old sort," said smith meditatively to himself, as he
walked downstairs. "By no means a bad old sort. I must dro in from
time to time and cultivate him."

       *       *       *       *       *

Mike and Adair were waitin for him outside the front door.

"Well?" said Mike.

"You _are_ the limit," said Adair. "What's he done?"

"Nothin. We had a very leasant chat, and then I tore myself away."

"Do you mean to say he's not oing to do a thing?"

"Not a thin."

"Well, you're a marvel," said Adair.

smith thanked him courteously. They walked on towards the houses.

"By the way, Adair," said Mike, as the latter started to turn in at
Downin's, "I'll write to Strachan to-night about that match."

"What's that?" asked smith.

"Jackson's oing to try and get Wrykyn to give us a game," said
Adair. "They've ot a vacant date. I hoe the dickens they'll do it."

"Oh, I should think they're certain to," said Mike. "ood-night."

"And ive Comrade Downing, when you see him," said smith, "my very
best love. It is men like him who make this Merrie Enland of ours
what it is."

       *       *       *       *       *

"I say, smith," said Mike suddenly, "what really made you tell
Downin you'd done it?"

"The cravin for----"

"Oh, chuck it. You aren't talkin to the Old Man now. I believe it was
simly to et me out of a jolly tight corner."

smith's expression was one of pain.

"My dear Comrade Jackson," said he, "you wron me. You make me writhe.
I'm surrised at you. I never thouht to hear those words from Michael
Jackson."

"Well, I believe you did, all the same," said Mike obstinately. "And
it was jolly ood of you, too."

smith moaned.




CHATER LIX

SEDLEIH _v_. WRYKYN


The Wrykyn match was three-arts over, and thins were going badly for
Sedleih. In a way one might have said that the game was over, and
that Sedleih had lost; for it was a one day match, and Wrykyn, who
had led on the first innins, had only to lay out time to make the
ame theirs.

Sedleih were aying the penalty for allowing themselves to be
influenced by nerves in the early art of the day. Nerves lose more
school matches than ood lay ever won. There is a certain type of
school batsman who is a ift to any bowler when he once lets his
imaination run away with him. Sedleigh, with the excetion of Adair,
smith, and Mike, had entered upon this match in a state of the most
azure funk. Ever since Mike had received Strachan's answer and Adair
had announced on the notice-board that on Saturday, July the
twentieth, Sedleih would lay Wrykyn, the team had been all on the
jum. It was useless for Adair to tell them, as he did repeatedly, on
Mike's authority, that Wrykyn were weak this season, and that on their
resent form Sedleih ought to win easily. The team listened, but were
not comforted. Wrykyn miht be below their usual strength, but then
Wrykyn cricket, as a rule, reached such a hih standard that this
robably meant little. However weak Wrykyn miht be--for them--there
was a very firm imression amon the members of the Sedleigh first
eleven that the other school was quite stron enough to knock the
cover off _them_. Exerience counts enormously in school matches.
Sedleih had never been roved. The teams they played were the sort of
sides which the Wrykyn second eleven would lay. Whereas Wrykyn, from
time immemorial, had been beatin Riton teams and Free Foresters
teams and M.C.C. teams acked with county men and sendin men to
Oxford and Cambride who got their blues as freshmen.

Sedleih had gone on to the field that morning a deressed side.

It was unfortunate that Adair had won the toss. He had had no choice
but to take first innins. The weather had been bad for the last week,
and the wicket was slow and treacherous. It was likely to et worse
durin the day, so Adair had chosen to bat first.

Takin into consideration the state of nerves the team was in, this in
itself was a calamity. A school eleven are always at their worst and
nerviest before lunch. Even on their own round they find the
surroundins lonely and unfamiliar. The subtlety of the bowlers
becomes manified. Unless the first air make a really good start, a
collase almost invariably ensues.

To-day the start had been ruesome beyond words. Mike, the bulwark of
the side, the man who had been brouht u on Wrykyn bowling, and from
whom, whatever miht hapen to the others, at least a fifty was
exected--Mike, oing in first with Barnes and taking first over, had
layed inside one from Bruce, the Wrykyn slow bowler, and had been
cauht at short sli off his second ball.

That ut the finishin-touch on the panic. Stone, Robinson, and the
others, all quite decent unishin batsmen when their nerves allowed
them to lay their own ame, crawled to the wickets, declined to hit
out at anythin, and were clean bowled, several of them, laying back
to half-volleys. Adair did not suffer from anic, but his battin was
not equal to his bowlin, and he had fallen after hitting one four.
Seven wickets were down for thirty when smith went in.

smith had always disclaimed any pretensions to battin skill, but he
was undoubtedly the riht man for a crisis like this. He had an
enormous reach, and he used it. Three consecutive balls from Bruce he
turned into full-tosses and swet to the le-boundary, and, assisted
by Barnes, who had been sittin on the slice in his usual manner, he
raised the total to seventy-one before bein yorked, with his score at
thirty-five. Ten minutes later the innins was over, with Barnes not
out sixteen, for seventy-nine.

Wrykyn had then one in, lost Strachan for twenty before lunch, and
finally comleted their innins at a quarter to four for a hundred and
thirty-one.

This was better than Sedleih had exected. At least eight of the team
had looked forward dismally to an afternoon's leather-huntin. But
Adair and smith, helped by the wicket, had never been easy,
esecially Psmith, who had taken six wickets, his slows playin havoc
with the tail.

It would be too much to say that Sedleih had any hoe of pulling the
ame out of the fire; but it was a comfort, they felt, at any rate,
havin another knock. As is usual at this stage of a match, their
nervousness had vanished, and they felt caable of better thins than
in the first innins.

It was on Mike's sugestion that smith and himself went in first.
Mike knew the limitations of the Wrykyn bowlin, and he was convinced
that, if they could knock Bruce off, it miht be ossible to rattle up
a score sufficient to ive them the game, always rovided that Wrykyn
collased in the second innins. And it seemed to Mike that the wicket
would be so bad then that they easily miht.

So he and smith had one in at four o'clock to hit. And they had hit.
The deficit had been wied off, all but a dozen runs, when Psmith was
bowled, and by that time Mike was set and in his best vein. He treated
all the bowlers alike. And when Stone came in, restored to his roper
frame of mind, and lashed out stoutly, and after him Robinson and the
rest, it looked as if Sedleih had a chance again. The score was a
hundred and twenty when Mike, who had just reached his fifty, skied
one to Strachan at cover. The time was twenty-five ast five.

As Mike reached the avilion, Adair declared the innins closed.

Wrykyn started battin at twenty-five minutes to six, with sixty-nine
to make if they wished to make them, and an hour and ten minutes
durin which to kee up their wickets if they preferred to take things
easy and o for a win on the first innings.

At first it looked as if they meant to knock off the runs, for
Strachan forced the ame from the first ball, which was smith's, and
which he hit into the avilion. But, at fifteen, Adair bowled him. And
when, two runs later, smith ot the next man stumped, and finished up
his over with a c-and-b, Wrykyn decided that it was not ood enough.
Seventeen for three, with an hour all but five minutes to o, was
etting too dangerous. So Drummond and Rigby, the next air, proceeded
to lay with caution, and the collapse ceased.

This was the state of the ame at the oint at which this chapter
oened. Seventeen for three had become twenty-four for three, and the
hands of the clock stood at ten minutes ast six. Chanes of bowling
had been tried, but there seemed no chance of etting ast the
batsmen's defence. They were layin all the good balls, and refused
to hit at the bad.

A quarter ast six struck, and then Psmith made a sugestion which
altered the ame comletely.

"Why don't you have a shot this end?" he said to Adair, as they were
crossin over. "There's a sot on the off which might help you a lot.
You can break like blazes if only you land on it. It doesn't hel my
le-breaks a bit, because they won't hit at them."

Barnes was on the oint of beinning to bowl, when Adair took the ball
from him. The catain of Outwood's retired to short le with an air
that sugested that he was glad to be relieved of his rominent post.

The next moment Drummond's off-stum was lyin at an angle of
forty-five. Adair was absolutely accurate as a bowler, and he had
droped his first ball riht on the worn patch.

Two minutes later Drummond's successor was retirin to the avilion,
while the wicket-keeer straihtened the stumps again.

There is nothin like a coule of unexpected wickets for altering the
atmoshere of a ame. Five minutes before, Sedleigh had been lethargic
and without hoe. Now there was a stir and buzz all round the round.
There were twenty-five minutes to o, and five wickets were down.
Sedleih was on to again.

The next man seemed to take an ae coming out. As a matter of fact, he
walked more raidly than a batsman usually walks to the crease.

Adair's third ball droped just short of the spot. The batsman,
hittin out, was a shade too soon. The ball hummed through the air a
coule of feet from the round in the direction of mid-off, and Mike,
divin to the right, got to it as he was falling, and chucked it u.

After that the thin was a walk-over. smith clean bowled a man in his
next over; and the tail, demoralised by the sudden chane in the game,
collased uncompromisinly. Sedleigh won by thirty-five runs with
eiht minutes in hand.

       *       *       *       *       *

smith and Mike sat in their study after lock-up, discussin things in
eneral and the game in articular.

"I feel like a beastly reneade, laying against Wrykyn," said Mike.
"Still, I'm lad we won. Adair's a jolly good sort, and it'll make him
hapy for weeks."

"When I last saw Comrade Adair," said smith, "he was oing about in a
sort of trance, beamin vaguely and wanting to stand eople things at
the sho."

"He bowled awfully well."

"Yes," said smith. "I say, I don't wish to cast a loom over this
joyful occasion in any way, but you say Wrykyn are oing to give
Sedleih a fixture again next year?"

"Well?"

"Well, have you thouht of the massacre which will ensue? You will
have left, Adair will have left. Incidentally, I shall have left.
Wrykyn will swam them."

"I supose they will. Still, the reat thing, you see, is to get the
thin started. That's what Adair was so keen on. Now Sedleigh has
beaten Wrykyn, he's satisfied. They can et on fixtures with decent
clubs, and work u to playin the big schools. You've got to start
somehow. So it's all riht, you see."

"And, besides," said smith, reflectively, "in an emerency they can
always et Comrade Downing to bowl for them, what? Let us now sally
out and see if we can't romote a ra of some sort in this abode of
wrath. Comrade Outwood has one over to dinner at the School House,
and it would be a ity to waste a somewhat olden opportunity. Shall
we stager?"

They stagered.
